Death of the Preemptor: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Inheritance of Shuf'ah
Dispute on inheritance of shuf'ah upon preemptor's death before judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the death of the preemptor before judgment, identify the condition of continuous ownership, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on analogy to khiyar al-shart.

Core Idea
The right of preemption is personal and conditional on continuous ownership; death severs it, so it is not inherited.

In this passage, al-Quduri states the Hanafi rule: if the preemptor dies before the judge's ruling, his right of preemption becomes void. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the right is inherited like an option. The commentator (Sahib al-Hidayah) clarifies the scope and provides the underlying principle: the preemptor must own the property from sale until judgment. This lesson examines the dispute, the condition, and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its scope
Identify the ruling: if the preemptor dies after the sale but before the judge's ruling, the right of preemption becomes void (batil). Note that if death occurs after the judgment but before payment, the right is binding for the heirs.
The scope is crucial: only death before qada' nullifies.
2
Identify the Shafi'i counter-position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the right of preemption is inherited like any other property right, even if the preemptor dies before judgment.
3
Locate the Hanafi dalil (condition of continuous ownership)
The Hanafi principle: the preemptor must own the property at the time of sale and this ownership must continue until the judge's ruling. Death severs ownership, so the condition is broken.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of ownership.
4
Examine the Shafi'i analogy to khiyar al-shart and the Hanafi response
Shafi'i analogizes shuf'ah to khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation), which is inherited. Hanafi responds that in khiyar al-shart ownership is suspended, not terminated, whereas in shuf'ah ownership must be continuous; death ends it.
The Hanafi response rejects the analogy due to a difference in the effective cause (illa).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Practical difference: if the preemptor dies before judgment, Hanafi says heirs have no right; Shafi'i says they can claim. The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi).

Source Text

حق شفعہ میں میراث جاری ہوگی یا نہیں
وهــذا نــــظـــــــر الاختلاف فــــى خـيـــــــــار الشـــــــــرط وقـد مـــــــرفـــــــى البيــــوع ترجمہ اور یہ خیار شرط کے اندر اختلاف کی نظیر ہے اور تحقیق کہ وہ بیوع میں گزر چکا ہے۔ تشریح یعنی حق شفعہ میں میراث جاری ہوگی یا نہیں۔ یہاں کا اختلاف بالکل ایسا ہے جو کتاب البیوع میں خیار شرط کے اندر گذر چکا ہے کہ امام شافعی کے نزدیک جیسے خیار شرط میں میراث جاری ہوتی ہے حق شفعہ کے اندر بھی ہوگی۔

تشریح اب یہاں یہ سوال پیدا ہوتا ہے کہ شفیع کے مرنے سے شفعہ کیوں باطل ہو جاتا ہے؟ تو اس کیلئے ایک اصول سمجھئے شفیع جس گھر کی وجہ سے شفعہ طلب کرے تو ضروری ہے کہ شفیع اس گھر کا مالک بیع کے وقت سے ہو اور یہ ملکیت کم از کم قضاء قاضی تک باقی رہنی چاہئے۔ تو چونکہ شفیع کے مرنے سے اس کی ملکیت زائل ہوگئی تو ملک کا بقاء نہیں پایا گیا۔ اسلئے شفعہ باطل ہو گیا اور ر ہے وارثین تو اگر چہ بوقت قضاء ان کی ملکیت موجود ہے مگر بوقت بیع ان کی ملکیت نہیں تھی اس وجہ سے وارثین کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا۔
Book of Preemption. He said: When the preemptor dies, his right of preemption becomes void. And al-Shafi'i said: It is inherited. Translation: Al-Quduri said: When the preemptor dies, his preemption becomes void, and al-Shafi'i said it is distributed as inheritance. Commentary: That is, if the preemptor dies and the judge has not yet ruled for him, his preemption becomes void. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, his preemption goes to his heirs as inheritance. What is the ruling if the preemptor dies after the sale but before or after the judge's ruling? He (may Allah be pleased with him) said: Its meaning is that if he dies after the sale before judgment of preemption, but if he dies after the judge's ruling before payment of the price and possession, then the sale is binding for his heirs. Translation: The author (Sahib al-Hidayah) said: The meaning is when the preemptor dies after the sale and before the judgment of preemption. However, if he dies after the judge's ruling before payment of the price and taking possession, then the sale is binding for the preemptor's heirs. Commentary: Sahib al-Hidayah says that the preemption becomes void by the death of the preemptor only when he dies after the sale and before the judge's ruling. If he dies after the judge's ruling, then the preemptor's heirs will have the right of preemption. Will inheritance apply in the right of preemption or not? And this is analogous to the difference in khiyar al-shart, and it has been mentioned in the Book of Sales. Translation: And this is analogous to the difference regarding the option of stipulation, and that has been discussed in the Book of Sales. Commentary: That is, will inheritance apply in the right of preemption? The difference here is exactly like that which passed in the Book of Sales regarding khiyar al-shart: According to Imam al-Shafi'i, just as inheritance applies in khiyar al-shart, it will also apply in the right of preemption. And according to the Hanafis, just as inheritance does not apply there, similarly it does not apply in the right of preemption. Principle regarding the nullification of preemption upon the preemptor's death: And because by death his ownership of his house is removed, and ownership is established for the heir after the sale, and its existence at the time of sale and its continuation for the preemptor until the time of judgment is a condition, so he does not deserve preemption without it. Commentary: Now a question arises here: Why does preemption become void upon the death of the preemptor? For this, understand a principle: The preemptor must be the owner of the house for which he seeks preemption from the time of the sale, and this ownership must remain at least until the judge's ruling. So since by the preemptor's death his ownership is removed, the continuity of ownership is not found. Therefore, preemption becomes void. As for the heirs, even if their ownership exists at the time of judgment, they did not have ownership at the time of sale, so the heirs will not receive preemption.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding whether the right of preemption (shuf'ah) is inherited upon the death of the preemptor. The Hanafi position, stated by al-Quduri in the matn, is that the right becomes void if the preemptor dies before the judge's ruling. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the right passes to the heirs as inheritance. The commentator, Sahib al-Hidayah, clarifies that this ruling applies only when death occurs after the sale but before the judge's ruling; if death occurs after the ruling but before payment, the sale becomes binding for the heirs. The dispute is analogous to that regarding khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) in the Book of Sales, where the Hanafis do not allow inheritance because the option is a personal right that does not survive death, while Shafi'is allow it. The Hanafi principle is that for shuf'ah to be established, the preemptor must be the owner of the property at the time of sale, and this ownership must continue until the judge's ruling. Death severs ownership, so the right is nullified. Heirs do not inherit it because they were not owners at the time of sale.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of preemption in property, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold share.
شفيع (shafi'): The person entitled to preemption (preemptor).
قضاء (qada'): The judge's ruling or judgment establishing the right.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The option of stipulation in a sale contract, allowing rescission within a fixed period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the preemptor dies after the sale but before the judge's ruling, what happens to the right of preemption?
A) It passes to the heirs.
B) It is nullified.
C) It becomes the buyer's right.
D) It is suspended until the heirs decide.

Key Takeaway
The right of preemption is not inherited because its validity depends on the preemptor's continuous ownership from the sale until the judge's ruling; death severs that condition.

Death of the Buyer Does Not Nullify the Right of Preemption
Ruling with rational proof on buyer's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of the buyer's death on the right of preemption and explain the rational basis for its continuity.

Core Idea
The preemptor's right outlives the buyer—it is not nullified by death, debt, or disposition.

This passage clarifies a crucial point in the law of preemption: the death of the buyer does not extinguish the preemptor's right. Hanafi scholars reason that since the preemptor (mustahiqq) and the cause of his right (the sale and his adjacency) remain unchanged, the right continues. The text then demonstrates the strength of this right by showing it prevails over creditors, legatees, and even the buyer's post-death transformations of the property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage begins by stating that the right of preemption (shuf'ah) is not nullified by the buyer's death. This is the central ruling.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason given: the preemptor (mustahiqq) remains, and the cause (sabab) of his right—the sale and his connection to the property—has not changed. The right is attached to the property and the claimant, not to the buyer's person.
This rational proof is the basis for all subsequent applications.
3
Apply to debts and bequests
If the buyer dies with debts, the preempted house cannot be sold to satisfy those debts. Instead, the preemptor takes the house and pays its price, which then goes to creditors. Similarly, a bequest (wasiyyah) cannot force the sale of the house; the preemptor's right is superior.
The preemptor's right is given priority over financial claims on the estate.
4
Apply to dispositions by judge or executor
If the judge (qadi) or executor (wasi) sells the house, or if the buyer bequeathed it as a mosque, the preemptor can nullify those dispositions and take the house. The preemptor's right is so strong that it overrides even court-ordered sales or religious endowments.
The passage emphasizes that the preemptor's right takes precedence (taqaddum).
5
Apply to physical transformation
Even if the buyer physically transformed the house into a mosque, graveyard, or waqf, the preemptor can reclaim it as long as the transformation is not complete in the sense of being irrevocable. The maxim that a mosque remains a mosque forever does not apply here because the house has not actually become a mosque in legal perpetuity.
The preemptor's right is so strong it can overturn even significant physical changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتری کے مرنے سے حق شفعہ باطل نہیں ہوتا
وان مات المشترى لم تبطل لان المستحق باق ولم يتغير سبب حقه ولا يباع في دين المشترى ووصيته ولو باعه القاضي او الوصى او اوصى المشترى فيها بوصية فللشفيع ان يبطله وياخذ الدار لتقدم حقه ولهذا ينقض تصرفه في حياته.
ترجمہ اور اگر مشتری مرگیا تو شفعہ باطل نہ ہوگا سلئے کہ مستحق باقی ہے اور اس کے حق کا سبب صغیر نہیں ہوا اور ( دار مشفوعہ ) کو بیچا نہیں با سکتا مشتری کے قرض میں اور اسکی وصیت میں اور اگر قاضی نے اس کو بیچ دیا ہو یا وصی نے یا مشتری نے اس میں کوئی وصیت کر دی ہو تو شفیع کیلئے حق ہے کہ اس کو باطل کر دے اور گھر کو لے لے شفیع کے حق کے مقدم ہونے کی وجہ سے اور اسی وجہ سے توڑ دیا جاتا ہے شفیع کا تصرف اس کی حیات میں۔ تشریح اگر مشتری مر گیا ہو تو چونکہ مستحق شفیع موجود ہے اور شفیع کا سبب اتصال تابید و قرار موجود ہے جس میں کچھ تغیر نہیں ہوا۔ لہذا یہاں شفعہ باطل نہ ہو گا۔ مشتری مر گیا اور اس کے ذمہ قرض ہے تو مکان کو بیچ کر قرض کی ادائیگی نہ ہوگی بلکہ مکان شفیع کو ملے گا اور جو رقم شفیع سے ملے اس سے قرض کی ادائیگی ہوگی۔
مشتری نے وصیت کی تھی کہ میرے اتنے روپے مسجد میں لگا دینا اور پے ہیں نہیں تو مکان فروخت کر کے وصیت پوری نہیں کی جائے گی بلکہ مکان شفیع کو ملے گا اور جور تم شفیع سے ملے حکم فقیہ کے مطابق وصیت پوری کی جاسکتی ہے۔
قاضی نے مکان کو فروخت کر دیا یا وصی نے فروخت کر دیا یا مشتری نے وصیت کی ہو کہ میرا یہ گھر مسجد بنا دینا۔ ان تمام صورتوں میں شفیع ان تصرفات کو باطل کر کے مکان کو لے سکتا ہے کیونکہ شفیع کا حق مقدم ہے اور اگر مشتری خود زندہ ہوتا تو اس کے تصرفات توڑ دیئے جاتے ہیں۔
اس طرح قاضی اور وصی کے تصرفات بھی توڑ دئیے جائیں گے۔ یہاں تک کہ اگر مشتری نے مکان کو خرید کر اس کی مسجد یا قبر سان بنا دیا یا وقف کر دیا اب شفیع کو بیع کا علم ہوا ہو ۔ اب بھی مشتری کے یہ جملہ تصرفات توڑ دیئے جائیں گے اور یہ مکان شفیع کو ملے گا۔ اور رہا یہ مسئلہ کہ مسجد قیامت تک مسجد ہی رہتی ہے یہ اس وقت ہے جبکہ وہ مسجد بن گئی ہو اور یہاں وہ گھر مسجد بنا ہی نہیں ہے۔
The right of preemption is not nullified by the death of the buyer. And if the buyer dies, it is not nullified because the claimant remains and the cause of his right has not changed, and it cannot be sold for the buyer's debt or bequest. If the judge or executor sells it, or the buyer makes a bequest regarding it, then the preemptor has the right to nullify that and take the house due to the precedence of his right, and for this reason his disposition during his life is invalidated. Translation: And if the buyer dies, preemption is not nullified because the claimant remains and the cause of his right has not changed, and the preempted house cannot be sold for the buyer's debt or his bequest. If the judge or executor sold it, or the buyer made a bequest in it, then the preemptor has the right to nullify that and take the house due to the precedence of his right, and for this reason the preemptor's disposition during his life is invalidated. Commentary: If the buyer dies, since the claimant preemptor exists and the cause of the preemptor's connection to the property remains unchanged, therefore preemption is not nullified. The buyer died and he has a debt; the house will not be sold to pay the debt, rather the house will go to the preemptor, and the money received from the preemptor will be used to pay the debt. The buyer had bequeathed that such and such money be spent on a mosque, but there is no cash, so the house will not be sold to fulfill the bequest; rather the house will go to the preemptor, and the money received from the preemptor can be used to fulfill the bequest according to the jurist's ruling. If the judge sold the house, or the executor sold it, or the buyer bequeathed that this house be made into a mosque, in all these cases the preemptor can nullify these dispositions and take the house, because the preemptor's right takes precedence. And if the buyer himself were alive, his dispositions would be overturned. Similarly, the dispositions of the judge and executor will be overturned. Even if the buyer built a mosque or graveyard on the house or endowed it, and later the preemptor learned of the sale, even then all these dispositions of the buyer will be overturned and the house will go to the preemptor. As for the issue that a mosque remains a mosque until the Day of Judgment, that applies when it has become a mosque, but here the house has not become a mosque.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the effect of the buyer's (mushtari) death on the right of preemption. The Hanafi school holds that the right of preemption is not nullified by the buyer's death. The reason is that the preemptor (mustahiqq) continues to exist, and the cause of his right (the sale and his connection to the property) remains unchanged. Therefore, the preemptor can still claim the property. The commentary then addresses several scenarios: If the buyer dies with debts, the house cannot be sold to satisfy those debts; instead, the preemptor takes the house and pays its price, and that money is used for the debts. Similarly, if the buyer made a bequest (wasiyyah), the house is not sold to fulfill it; the preemptor takes the house and pays the price, which is then used for the bequest according to the jurist's determination. If the judge or executor sells the house after the buyer's death, or if the buyer bequeathed that the house be made into a mosque, the preemptor can nullify all these dispositions and take the house, because his right takes precedence. Even if the buyer physically transformed the house into a mosque, graveyard, or waqf, the preemptor can still nullify these actions and reclaim the house, as long as the transformation is not complete (i.e., the house has not actually become a mosque in perpetuity). The passage emphasizes the strength of the preemptor's right over that of creditors, legatees, and even over the buyer's own dispositions, both during his life and after death.

Key Terms
مستحق (mustahiqq): The person entitled to the right, i.e., the preemptor.
مشترى (mushtari): The buyer of the property subject to preemption.
قاضي (qadi): The judge who may sell the property if necessary.
وصي (wasi): The executor of the buyer's will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the buyer dies after purchasing the property, the preemptor's right:
A) Is nullified.
B) Remains and can overturn the buyer's estate.
C) Passes to the buyer's heirs.
D) Is limited to half the property.

Key Takeaway
The right of preemption survives the buyer's death and remains enforceable against the estate, creditors, legatees, and even against dispositions that transform the property, due to the continued existence of the claimant and the cause of his right.

Death of the Preemptor Voids Shuf'a
Explains via talil why death voids shuf'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of ownership continuity for shuf'a and apply it to determine whether the right is voided by the preemptor's death.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is void if the preemptor's ownership ends before the court ruling — death breaks the chain.

The right of preemption (shuf'a) is not absolute; it depends on the preemptor's continuous ownership of the property that gives him the right. This lesson examines a key ruling: why the preemptor's death before the court ruling voids shuf'a completely, and why heirs cannot claim it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the preemptor's claim of shuf'a; the ruling is that it becomes void (batil) upon his death before the court ruling.
2
Locate the condition
The key condition is ownership continuity (ittisal): the preemptor must own the adjacent property from the time of the sale until the judge's ruling.
This condition is explicitly stated in the passage.
3
Apply the condition to the case
Death ends the preemptor's ownership, so the continuity condition is broken. Therefore shuf'a is void.
4
Distinguish heirs
Heirs, though owners at the time of the ruling, were not owners at the time of the sale, so the condition is not met for them. Hence they cannot claim shuf'a.
This is the specific conclusion for the scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة
1.4
اشرف الهدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ۱۳
تشریح اب یہاں یہ سوال پیدا ہوتا ہے کہ شفیع کے مرنے سے شفعہ کیوں باطل ہو جاتا ہے؟ تو اس کیلئے ایک اصول سمجھئے شفیع جس گھر کی وجہ سے شفعہ طلب کرے تو ضروری ہے کہ شفیع اس گھر کا مالک بیع کے وقت سے ہو اور یہ ملکیت کم از کم قضاء قاضی تک باقی رہنی چاہئے۔ تو چونکہ شفیع کے مرنے سے اس کی ملکیت زائل ہوگئی تو ملک کا بقاء نہیں پایا گیا۔ اسلئے شفعہ باطل ہو گیا اور ر ہے وارثین تو اگر چہ بوقت قضاء ان کی ملکیت موجود ہے مگر بوقت بیع ان کی ملکیت نہیں تھی اس وجہ سے وارثین کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا۔
Chapter of Preemption. 1.4 Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, volume 13. Explanation: Now here a question arises: why does shuf'a become void upon the death of the preemptor? For this, understand a principle: the preemptor, by virtue of which house he claims preemption, it is necessary that he be the owner of that house from the time of the sale and that this ownership remain at least until the judge's ruling. So, since with the death of the preemptor his ownership ceased, the continuity of ownership was not found. Therefore, shuf'a became void. As for the heirs, although their ownership exists at the time of the ruling, their ownership did not exist at the time of the sale, so the heirs will not receive shuf'a.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explanation (talil) for why the right of preemption (shuf'a) is voided by the death of the preemptor. The key principle is that the preemptor must own the property that gives him the right (the adjacent or shared property) both at the time of the sale of the subject property and continuously until the judge's ruling. Death removes ownership, so the right is lost. Heirs do not inherit this right because they were not owners at the time of the sale.

Key Terms
شفیع (shafi'): The preemptor: the person entitled to claim preemption due to ownership of adjacent or shared property.
شفعہ (shuf'a): The right of preemption: the right to take ownership of a sold property from the buyer by paying the price.
اتصال (ittisal): Continuous connection: the requirement that the preemptor's ownership of the adjacent property be uninterrupted from the time of sale until the court ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does shuf'a become void when the preemptor dies before the court ruling?
A) Because the preemptor's heirs do not consent.
B) Because the preemptor's ownership of the adjacent property is lost and the right depends on its continuity.
C) Because the buyer dies.
D) Because the judge cancels the sale.

Key Takeaway
The right of shuf'a is personal and conditional: it requires the preemptor's continuous ownership from sale to judgment; death severs that chain.

Death of the Purchaser Does Not Void Shuf'a
Ruling with causal reasoning (talil) for persistence of shuf'a after buyer's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the death of the purchaser does not void shuf'a, explain the causal reasoning (talil), and apply it to scenarios involving debts, bequests, and dispositions by qadi/executor.

Core Idea
The preemptor's right of shuf'a survives the buyer's death and prevails over debts, bequests, and even court-ordered sales.

Shuf'a (pre-emption) is a right to step into a sale of adjacent or co-owned property. In the Hanafi school, this right is robust: it is not extinguished by the buyer's death. In this lesson, we explore the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical implications, including how the preemptor can void subsequent dispositions made by the deceased buyer, his executor, or even the qadi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the opening sentence of the passage. The act is the death of the purchaser (mushtari). The ruling is that shuf'a does not become void (la tabṭul).
The ruling is a negation: the right of shuf'a remains valid (sahih) after the buyer's death.
2
Locate the causal reasoning (talil)
The passage gives the reason: 'because the one entitled (the preemptor) remains and his right's cause has not changed.' The cause (sabab) is the preemptor's ownership of adjacent property, which persists unchanged.
The preemptor's right is tied to his own property, not to the buyer's life.
3
Trace the implication for debts and bequests
The passage states that the purchased property cannot be sold for the purchaser's debt or to fulfill his bequest, because the preemptor's right takes precedence. The house must go to the preemptor first.
If a debt exists, the preemptor pays the sale price, which is then used to settle the debt.
4
Analyze dispositions by qadi or executor
Even if the qadi (judge) or the executor (waṣi) sells the property, or the purchaser bequeaths it to become a mosque, the preemptor can void these dispositions.
The preemptor's right is superior; it overrides judicial and testamentary acts.
5
Evaluate the extreme case of mosque conversion
The commentary notes that if the buyer began building a mosque but it is not yet complete, the preemptor can still claim the property. The rule that a mosque remains a mosque only applies after it is fully built.
This distinguishes between mere intention/partial construction and completed religious endowment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتری کے مرنے سے حق شفعہ باطل نہیں ہوتا
وان مات المشترى لم تبطل لان المستحق باق ولم يتغير سبب حقه ولا يباع في دين المشترى ووصيته ولو باعه القاضي او الوصى او اوصى المشترى فيها بوصية فللشفيع ان يبطله وياخذ الدار لتقدم حقه ولهذا ينقض تصرفه في حياته.
ترجمہ اور اگر مشتری مرگیا تو شفعہ باطل نہ ہوگا اسلئے کہ مستحق باقی ہے اور اس کے حق کا سبب تغیر نہیں ہوا اور ( دار مشفوعہ ) کو بیچا نہیں جا سکتا مشتری کے قرض میں اور اسکی وصیت میں اور اگر قاضی نے اس کو بیچ دیا ہو یا وصی نے یا مشتری نے اس میں کوئی وصیت کر دی ہو تو شفیع کیلئے حق ہے کہ اس کو باطل کر دے اور گھر کو لے لے شفیع کے حق کے مقدم ہونے کی وجہ سے اور اسی وجہ سے توڑ دیا جاتا ہے شفیع کا تصرف اس کی حیات میں۔

مشتری نے وصیت کی تھی کہ میرے اتنے روپے مسجد میں لگا دینا اور پے ہیں نہیں تو مکان فروخت کر کے وصیت پوری نہیں کی جائے گی بلکہ مکان شفیع کو ملے گا اور جو رقم شفیع سے ملے حکم فقیہ کے مطابق وصیت پوری کی جاسکتی ہے۔
قاضی نے مکان کو فروخت کر دیا یا وصی نے فروخت کر دیا یا مشتری نے وصیت کی ہو کہ میرا یہ گھر مسجد بنا دینا۔ ان تمام صورتوں میں شفیع ان تصرفات کو باطل کر کے مکان کو لے سکتا ہے کیونکہ شفیع کا حق مقدم ہے اور اگر مشتری خود زندہ ہوتا تو اس کے تصرفات توڑ دیئے جاتے ہیں۔
اس طرح قاضی اور وصی کے تصرفات بھی توڑ دئیے جائیں گے۔ یہاں تک کہ اگر مشتری نے مکان کو خرید کر اس کی مسجد یا قبرستان بنا دیا یا وقف کر دیا اب شفیع کو بیع کا علم ہوا ہو۔ اب بھی مشتری کے یہ جملہ تصرفات توڑ دئیے جائیں گے اور یہ مکان شفیع کو ملے گا۔ اور رہا یہ مسئلہ کہ مسجد قیامت تک مسجد ہی رہتی ہے یہ اس وقت ہے جبکہ وہ مسجد بن گئی ہو اور یہاں وہ گھر مسجد بنا ہی نہیں ہے۔
The right of shuf'a does not become void by the death of the purchaser. And if the purchaser dies, it does not become void because the one entitled (the preemptor) remains and his right's cause has not changed. And it (the purchased property) cannot be sold for the purchaser's debt or his bequest. And if the qadi sold it, or the executor, or the purchaser made a bequest regarding it, then the preemptor has the right to void that and take the house due to the precedence of his right. And for this reason, his (the preemptor's) action (to claim) is effective during his life. Translation: And if the purchaser died, then shuf'a will not become void because the one entitled remains and the cause of his right has not changed. And the purchased house cannot be sold for the purchaser's debt or his bequest. And if the qadi sold it, or the executor, or the purchaser made a bequest, then the preemptor has the right to void it and take the house due to the precedence of his right. And for this reason, the preemptor's claim is effective during his life. Commentary: If the purchaser died, since the preemptor exists and his cause of connection remains unchanged, therefore shuf'a will not become void. If the purchaser died and he had a debt, the house will not be sold to pay the debt; rather the house will go to the preemptor, and the money from the preemptor will be used to pay the debt. If the purchaser had bequeathed that a certain amount be spent on the mosque, and there is no cash, the house will not be sold to fulfill the bequest; rather the house will go to the preemptor, and the money from the preemptor can be used for the bequest according to the ruling of the jurist. If the qadi sold the house or the executor sold it, or the purchaser bequeathed that his house be made into a mosque, in all these situations the preemptor can void those actions and take the house because the preemptor's right takes precedence. And if the purchaser were alive, his actions would be voided. Similarly, the actions of the qadi and executor will be voided. Even if the purchaser, after buying, made it into a mosque or graveyard or endowment, and then the preemptor learned of the sale, even then all these actions of the purchaser will be voided and the house will go to the preemptor. As for the rule that a mosque remains a mosque until the Day of Judgment, that applies when the mosque is actually built; here the house has not become a mosque.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil) that the death of the purchaser (mushtari) does not void the right of shuf'a. The reason is that the preemptor (shafi') is still alive and the cause of his right (his connection to the adjacent property) remains unchanged. The passage also clarifies that the purchased property cannot be used to pay the purchaser's debts or fulfill his bequests; rather, the preemptor's right takes precedence. Even if the qadi, executor, or the purchaser himself (via bequest) attempts to dispose of the property, the preemptor can void those dispositions. The commentary adds that even if the purchaser had turned the property into a mosque or graveyard, but construction is not yet complete, the preemptor can still claim it. The Hanafi school emphasizes the precedence of the preemptor's right.

Key Terms
مشتری (mushtari): The purchaser/buyer of the property subject to preemption.
مستحق (mustahiq): The entitled person, i.e., the preemptor (shafi').
سبب (sabab): The cause of the right: the preemptor's ownership of the adjacent property (ittisal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Yes, because the preemptor's right remains and takes precedence over debts and bequests.

D) Only if the preemptor pays double the price.

Key Takeaway
The preemptor's right of shuf'a is independent of the buyer's life; it persists after the buyer's death and can nullify subsequent transactions.

Preemptor Selling His Own Property Voids Shuf'a
Ruling with proof by reasoning (talil) about shuf'a being void when cause is removed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that shuf'a becomes void when the preemptor sells his adjacent property before the court ruling, and explain the exception for khiyar.

Core Idea
Selling the adjacent property removes the cause of shuf'a, voiding the right even if the preemptor was unaware.

The right of shuf'a (preemption) is not absolute—it depends on a continuous cause: your ownership of the adjacent property. If you sell that property before the judge awards you the subject property, the cause disappears and your right vanishes. This holds even if you didn't know about the original sale. However, if you sell with a khiyar (option) that keeps ownership in your hands, the right remains. This passage from al-Quduri, as explained in al-Hidayah, establishes a key principle in Hanafi property law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and resultant ruling
What did the preemptor do? He sold his own adjacent property before the court ruled on his shuf'a claim. The ruling: his right of shuf'a becomes void (batil).
The act is selling the property that gives him the right.
2
Locate the cause (sabab) of shuf'a
The cause of entitlement is the 'connection' (ittisal) to the preemptor's property—his ownership of the adjacent land or house.
Without this ownership, the right cannot exist.
3
Understand the removal of the cause
By selling, the preemptor transfers ownership of his property, so the connection is severed. Since the cause is removed before he acquires the subject property, the right is void.
This is true even if he was unaware of the original sale—ignorance does not preserve the right.
4
Compare with explicit relinquishment
The ruling is analogous to explicit dast-bardari (relinquishment) or discharging a debt without knowledge: the right is lost regardless of knowledge.
This emphasizes that the right depends on objective legal presence, not subjective knowledge.
5
Identify the exception (khiyar)
If the preemptor sells with a condition of khiyar for himself, ownership does not transfer during the option period. Thus the connection remains, and shuf'a is preserved.
Khiyar al-ba'i keeps the property in the seller's ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا باع الشفيع ما يشفع به قبل ان يقضى له بالشفعة بطلت شفعته لزوال سبب الاستحقاق قبل التملك وهو الاتصال بملكه ولهذا يزول به وان لم يعلم بشراء المشفوعة كما اذا سلم صريحا او ابرأ عن الدين وهو لا يعلم به وهذا بخلاف ما اذا باع الشفيع داره بشرط الخيار له لانه يمنع الزوال فبقى الاتصال
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جبکہ شفیع نے وہ گھر فروخت کر دیا جس کی وجہ سے وہ شفیع بنا تھا اس کیلئے شفعہ کا فیصلہ کیئے جانے سے پہلے تو اس کا شفعہ باطل ہو گیا مالک بننے سے پہلے ہی استحقاق کا سبب زائل ہونے کی وجہ سے اور وہ شفیع کی ملکیت کے ساتھ اتصال ہے اور اسی وجہ سے زائل ہو جائے گا۔ حق شفعہ اس سے ( شفیع کے اپنا گھر بیچنے کی وجہ سے اگر چہ وہ نہ جانے دار مشفوعہ کی شراء کو جیسا کہ جبکہ شفیع نے صراحۂ دست برداری دے دی ہو یا اس نے قرض سے بری کر دیا ہو۔ حالانکہ وہ دین کو نہیں جانتا اور یہ اس صورت کے خلاف ہے جبکہ شفیع نے اپنا گھر فروخت کیا ہو اپنے لئے خیار کی شرط کے ساتھ اسلئے کہ یہ (خیار بائع ) زوال کو روکتا ہے تو اتصال باقی ہے۔
تشریح ابھی تک شفیع کیلئے قاضی نے شفعہ کا فیصلہ نہیں کیا تھا کہ شفیع نے اپنا وہ گھر ہی فروخت کر دیا جس کی بنیاد پر اس کو حق شفعہ پہنچتا ہے تو اب اس کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا۔ اسلئے کہ ابھی تک دار مشفوعہ میں شفیع کی ملکیت ثابت نہیں ہوئی تھی اور جو استحقاق شفعہ کا سبب تھا وہ بھی زائل ہو گیا۔ لہذا شفعہ باطل ہو گیا کیونکہ سبب استحقاق وہ اتصال تابید وقرار تھا جو ختم ہو چکا ہے۔
ولهذا يزول به ... الخ شفیع کے پڑوس کا مکان فروخت ہو گیا مگر اس کو اس بیع وشراء کا علم نہ ہوسکا اور اس نے اپنا مکان فروخت کاعلم کر دیا اب اس کا شفعہ باطل ہو گیا۔ اور یہ بالکل ایسا ہے جیسا کہ دار مشفوعہ کی بیع ہوئی اور شفیع کو علم نہ ہو سکا اور اس نے بیع کا علم نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے شفعہ سے دست برداری دے دی ہوتب بھی شفعہ باطل ہوتا ہے۔ ایسے ہی مثال سابق میں شفعہ باطل ہو جائے گا۔ او ابرا.... الخ زید پر بکر کا قرض ہے اور بکر کو اس کا علم نہیں اور لاعلمی کی وجہ سے اس نے زید کو بری کر دیا تو بس زید قرض سے بری ہو گیا۔ و هذا بخلاف ..... الخ شفیع نے اپنا مذ کورہ گھر فروخت کر دیا مگر شفیع نے اپنے لئے خیار شرط لیا ہے تو شفیع کو شفعہ ملے گا اسلئے کہ بائع کے خیار کی وجہ سے مبیع بائع کی ملکیت سے نہیں نکلتی، تو سبب استحقاق یعنی اتصال برقرار ہے۔
He (al-Quduri) said: And if the preemptor sells the property by virtue of which he has the right of preemption, before the court rules in his favor for the preemption, his preemption is void because the cause of entitlement is removed before ownership, and that cause is the connection to his property. And for this reason, it (the right) is removed even if he does not know of the purchase of the subject property, just as if he explicitly relinquished or discharged a debt without knowing about it. And this is unlike the case where the preemptor sells his house with a condition of option (khiyar) for himself, because that prevents removal, so the connection remains. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And when the preemptor sells the house by virtue of which he is a preemptor, before the ruling of shuf'a is made for him, then his shuf'a becomes void due to the cause of entitlement being removed before he becomes owner (of the subject property), and that cause is the connection to his property. And for this reason, it is removed by that (selling), even if he did not know of the purchase of the subject property, just as if he explicitly relinquished or discharged a debt without knowing it. And this is contrary to the case where the preemptor sells his house with a condition of option for himself, because that prevents removal, so the connection remains. Commentary: The qadi had not yet decided shuf'a for the preemptor, and the preemptor sold the very house on the basis of which he had the right of shuf'a. So now he will not get shuf'a. Because ownership in the subject property had not yet been established for him, and the cause of entitlement (the connection) had also been removed. Therefore shuf'a became void because the cause of entitlement – the continuous and stable connection – had ended. 'And for this reason it is removed by it...' etc.: The neighbor's house was sold, but he did not know of that sale and he sold his own house; now his shuf'a is void. This is exactly like when the subject property is sold and the preemptor does not know, and due to ignorance he relinquishes shuf'a; then also shuf'a is void. Similarly in the earlier example, shuf'a will be void. '...or discharged...' etc.: Zayd has a debt on Bakr, and Bakr does not know, and due to ignorance he discharges Zayd; then Zayd is free from the debt. 'And this is unlike...' etc.: The preemptor sold his aforementioned house, but he took a condition of option for himself; then the preemptor will get shuf'a because due to the seller's option, the sold property does not leave the seller's ownership, so the cause of entitlement – the connection – remains.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil) that if the preemptor sells his own property (the one that gives him the right of shuf'a) before the court ruling on his claim, his right of shuf'a becomes void. The reason is that the cause of his entitlement—his ownership of the adjacent property—is removed. This holds even if he was unaware of the sale of the subject property, similar to explicit relinquishment or discharge of a debt in ignorance. An exception is made if he sells with a condition of option (khiyar) for himself, because during the option period the property remains his, so the connection persists. The Urdu commentary elaborates with examples.

Key Terms
خیار (khiyar): Option: a condition in a sale that allows one party to rescind within a specified period. Here, khiyar al-ba'i (seller's option) prevents transfer of ownership.
دست برداری (dast-bardari): Relinquishment: explicitly giving up the right of shuf'a.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the right of shuf'a if the preemptor sells his adjacent property before the court ruling?
A) It remains valid because he was the owner at the time of the original sale.
B) It becomes void because the cause (connection) is removed.
C) It becomes void only if he knew about the sale of the subject property.
D) It remains valid if he sells with a condition of option for himself.

Key Takeaway
The right of shuf'a depends on the continuous ownership of the adjacent property; selling it before the court ruling extinguishes the right, except when a khiyar condition retains ownership.

Agent of Seller vs Agent of Buyer in Preemption
Ruling on whether an agent who is also the preemptor can claim shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for when an agent of the seller or buyer can claim shuf'a and apply it to new scenarios.

Core Idea
The right of preemption attaches to the buyer, not the seller; the agent's role follows the principal.

This lesson examines a specific rule from the Hanafi matn: whether a person who is both an agent in a property sale and a preemptor can claim shuf'a. The answer depends on which party the agent represents. Understanding this distinction clarifies the underlying principle that shuf'a belongs to the buyer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) a person is agent of the seller and also the preemptor, (2) a person is agent of the buyer and also the preemptor. Note that the same person is both agent and preemptor in each case.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For the agent of the seller: no shuf'a. For the agent of the buyer: shuf'a is established. Write down the rulings clearly.
The matn states these as unqualified rules.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The right of shuf'a (preemption) belongs to the buyer of the property. The agent of the seller is not the buyer; the sale is attributed to the principal. The agent of the buyer, however, stands in the place of the buyer for the transaction, so he acquires the right.
The agent of the buyer is effectively considered the buyer for purposes of shuf'a.
4
Apply to hypotheticals
Create new scenarios where the agent is also a co-owner or where the principal is also the preemptor. Determine whether shuf'a exists based on the rule.
Focus on who the 'buyer' is in the transaction.

Source Text

قال ووكيل البائع اذا باع وهو الشفيع فلا شفعة له ووكيل المشترى اذا ابتاع فله الشفعة
ترجمہ اہم قدوری نے فرمایا اور بائع کا وکیل جب بیچے اور وہی شفیع ہو تو اس کیلئے شفعہ نہیں ہے اور مشتری کا وکیل جب خریدے تو اس کیلئے شفعہ ہے۔
تشریح زید نے بکر کو وکیل بنایا کہ بکر اس کا مکان فروخت کر دے بکر نے کر دیا اتفاق سے شفیع بھی بکر ہے تو اب بکر کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا اور اگر زید نے بکر کو کوئی مکان خریدنے کیلئے وکیل بنایا اور اس نے خریدا اور اتفاق ہے شفیع بھی بکر ہی ہے تو اس صورت میں بکر کو حق شفعہ
He (al-Quduri) said: And the agent of the seller, when he sells while he is the preemptor, then there is no shuf'a for him. And the agent of the buyer, when he buys, then he has shuf'a. Translation: Important al-Quduri said: And the agent of the seller, when he sells and he is the preemptor, then there is no shuf'a for him. And the agent of the buyer, when he buys, then for him there is shuf'a. Commentary: Zayd made Bakr his agent to sell his house. Bakr sold it. By chance, Bakr is also the preemptor. Then Bakr will not get shuf'a. And if Zayd made Bakr his agent to buy a house, and he bought it, and by chance Bakr is also the preemptor, then in this case Bakr will get the right of shuf'a.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement (without explicit proof) from the matn. It distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) If a person is both the agent of the seller and the preemptor, he does not get shuf'a because the sale he conducted is attributed to the principal (seller), not to himself, so he is not the buyer. (2) If he is the agent of the buyer, he is considered the buyer in effect since the purchase is for his principal, but the preemptor's right attaches to the buyer; however, the agent is the one who executed the purchase, so he can claim shuf'a because he stands in the place of the buyer. The Urdu commentary illustrates with examples.

Key Terms
وکیل (wakil): Agent: a person authorized to act on behalf of another in a sale or purchase.
بائع (ba'i'): The seller.
مشتری (mushtari): The buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person acts as an agent for the seller in a property sale and is also the preemptor, does he get shuf'a?
A) Yes, because he is the one who sold.
B) No, because he is not the buyer; the right belongs to the buyer.
C) Yes, if he was unaware of his preemption right.
D) No, unless he also acts as agent for the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The agent of the seller cannot claim shuf'a; only the agent of the buyer can, because shuf'a belongs to the buyer.

Selling One's Own Property Before Decree Voids Shuf'ah
Ruling with talil on loss of shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the pre-emptor's right is void if he sells his property before the decree, and identify the exception when the sale includes a condition of option.

Core Idea
The right of shuf'ah requires continued connection; selling your property removes it.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). The key question: does the pre-emptor lose his right if he sells his own property before the judge has decreed the pre-emption? The answer, based on the principle that the cause of the right is the physical connection of properties, is that the right becomes void. We will also explore an important exception involving a sale with a condition of option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The act is: the pre-emptor (shafi') sells his own property (the one that gave him the right) before the decree for shuf'ah. The ruling: his right to pre-emption becomes void (batil).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is not a textual source but a legal reasoning (talil): the cause (sabab) of shuf'ah is the connection (ittisal) between the properties. Selling the property removes that cause before ownership of the pre-empted property is acquired.
The text uses 'li' (due to) to introduce the reasoning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof connects to the ruling)
The ruling follows logically: if the cause (connection) is removed, the right cannot be sustained. The text emphasizes that this holds even if the pre-emptor was unaware of the original sale, similar to explicit relinquishment.
Ignorance does not preserve the cause.
4
Note the exception
An exception is made when the pre-emptor sells his property with a condition of option (khiyar al-bay') for himself. In that case, the sale is not final until the option expires, so the connection remains, and the right is preserved.
The option prevents the property from fully leaving ownership.
5
Consider cross-madhhab differences
This ruling is specific to the Hanafi view of shuf'ah based on neighborly connection. Other schools may have different conditions that affect the outcome.
The passage does not elaborate, but the Hanafi position is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا باع الشفيع ما يشفع به قبل ان يقضى له بالشفعة بطلت شفعته لزوال سبب الاستحقاق قبل التملك وهو الاتصال بملكه ولهذا يزول به وان لم يعلم بشراء المشفوعة كما اذا سلم صريحا او ابرأ عن الدين وهو لا يعلم به وهذا بخلاف ما اذا باع الشفيع داره بشرط الخيار له لانه يمنع الزوال فبقى الاتصال
He said: And if the pre-emptor sells what he pre-empts by before judgment for him for pre-emption, his pre-emption becomes void due to the removal of the cause of entitlement before ownership, and it is the connection to his property, and due to this it is removed even if he did not know of the purchase of the pre-empted property, just as if he explicitly relinquished or discharged a debt while not knowing of it. And this is contrary to if the pre-emptor sold his house with a condition of option for himself, because it prevents removal, so the connection remains.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding the loss of the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). The ruling: If the pre-emptor (shafi') sells his own property (the one that gave him the right of pre-emption) before the judge has decreed the pre-emption in his favor, his right to pre-emption becomes void. The reason: The cause (sabab) of entitlement to pre-emption is the connection (ittisal) of the pre-emptor's property to the sold property, which gives him the right to substitute that connection. Since he has not yet acquired ownership of the pre-empted property (by decree), selling his own property removes the cause before ownership, thus voiding the right. The text further clarifies that this applies even if the pre-emptor was unaware of the original sale at the time he sold his property, similar to cases where he explicitly relinquishes his right (taslim sarih) or discharges a debt without knowing he owes it. An exception is made if the pre-emptor sells his house with a condition of option (khiyar al-bay') for himself. In such a case, his right remains because the property has not fully left his ownership until the option period ends, so the connection (ittisal) remains intact. This is a Hanafi ruling from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property sold to an outsider by substituting himself in the sale.
الشفيع (al-shafi'): The person entitled to pre-emption (pre-emptor).
اتصال (ittisal): Connection or contiguity of the pre-emptor's property to the sold property, which is the cause of the right of pre-emption.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option to cancel or confirm a sale. Here it refers to the seller's option (khiyar al-bay').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the right of pre-emption if the pre-emptor sells his own property before the judge decrees the pre-emption?
A) The right remains because it is a prior right.
B) The right is void because the cause (connection) is removed.
C) The right is suspended until the pre-emptor repurchases.
D) The right is transferred to the new buyer.

Key Takeaway
The right of shuf'ah depends on the continued connection of the pre-emptor's property; selling it before decree severs the cause and voids the right, unless the sale includes a condition of option for the pre-emptor.

Agent of Seller Has No Pre-emption
Ruling on the agent of the seller regarding pre-emption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the agent of the seller has no right of pre-emption and explain the reason based on his role in executing the sale.

Core Idea
The seller's agent cannot claim pre-emption because he caused the sale.

When a seller appoints an agent to sell property, the agent acts on his behalf. If that agent himself is the pre-emptor (e.g., adjacent owner), one might think he could still claim pre-emption. However, the Hanafi ruling is clear: once the agent executes the sale, he forfeits his right of pre-emption. Why? Because claiming pre-emption would mean nullifying his own transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actor
The actor is the agent of the seller (wakil al-ba'i'). He is appointed to sell the property.
2
Understand the act
The agent executes the sale on behalf of the seller. This act transfers ownership to the buyer.
3
Recognize his status
The agent himself is also the pre-emptor (shafi'), meaning he has rights in the property (e.g., adjacent owner or co-owner).
4
Apply the ruling
According to the Hanafi school, the agent has no right of pre-emption (la shuf'ata lahu).
5
Reason
If he claimed pre-emption, he would be nullifying a sale he himself executed. This is inconsistent; therefore, the right is lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ووكيل البائع اذا باع وهو الشفيع فلا شفعة له ووكيل المشترى اذا ابتاع فله الشفعة
He said: And the agent of the seller, when he sells while he is the pre-emptor, then there is no pre-emption for him. And the agent of the buyer, when he buys, then for him is the pre-emption.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents two cases regarding the effect of agency on the right of pre-emption. First case: If the agent of the seller (wakil al-ba'i') sells the property, and that agent himself is the pre-emptor (i.e., his own property is adjacent), then he does not have the right of pre-emption. The reasoning is that he is the one who executed the sale, so claiming pre-emption would mean nullifying his own action. Second case: If the agent of the buyer (wakil al-mushtari) purchases the property, and he is the pre-emptor, then he does have the right of pre-emption. This is because he is acting on behalf of the buyer; the sale was not his own act, so claiming pre-emption does not contradict his agency. This ruling is based on the general principle that one who sells cannot later claim pre-emption, while one who buys can. The commentary expands on this with examples.

Key Terms
وكيل البائع (wakil al-ba'i'): The agent appointed by the seller to sell the property.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption allowing an owner of adjacent or shared property to purchase it when sold to an outsider.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does the agent of the seller have the right of pre-emption when he sells the property?
A) Yes
B) No
C) Only if he is a co-owner
D) Only if he discloses his adjacency

Key Takeaway
The agent of the seller loses the right of pre-emption because he is the one who executed the sale.

Agent of Buyer Has Pre-emption
Ruling on the agent of the buyer regarding pre-emption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the agent of the buyer has the right of pre-emption and distinguish this case from the seller's agent.

Core Idea
The buyer's agent can claim pre-emption because he did not cause the sale.

The agent of the buyer purchases property on behalf of the buyer. If this agent himself is a pre-emptor (e.g., adjacent owner), the Hanafi school grants him the right of pre-emption. Unlike the seller's agent, he did not execute the sale in his own capacity, so claiming pre-emption does not contradict his agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actor
The actor is the agent of the buyer (wakil al-mushtari). He is appointed to buy the property.
2
Understand the act
The agent executes the purchase on behalf of the buyer. The sale is between the seller and the buyer; the agent is merely a representative.
3
Recognize his status
The agent himself is also the pre-emptor (shafi'), meaning he has rights in the property (e.g., adjacent owner).
4
Apply the ruling
According to the Hanafi school, the agent has the right of pre-emption (fahu al-shuf'ah).
5
Contrast with seller's agent
The buyer's agent did not cause the sale; he only facilitated it. Therefore, claiming pre-emption does not undermine his own action.

The reasoning is that he is the one who executed the sale, so claiming pre-emption would mean nullifying his own action. Guarantee Against Defects by Pre-emptor Voids Shuf'ah
Ruling statement: guarantee voids shuf'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a pre-emptor's guarantee against defects voids his right of pre-emption, and explain why this constitutes relinquishment.

Core Idea
Guaranteeing the seller against defects = accepting the sale; shuf'ah is lost.

We have seen that certain actions by the pre-emptor affirm the sale and waive his right. This passage introduces another such action: giving a guarantee (daman al-darak) to the seller. Understanding when shuf'ah is voided is crucial for both pre-emptors and sellers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The pre-emptor gives a guarantee (daman al-darak) to the seller against hidden defects or eviction.
2
Recognize the legal effect
The right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) becomes void (batil).
3
Understand the rationale
The guarantee implies consent to the sale; claiming pre-emption would contradict that consent.
4
Classify the consequence
This is a form of relinquishment (isqat) of the right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك لوضمن الدرك عن البائع وهو الشفيع فلا شفعة
And likewise, if he guarantees the freedom from defects against the seller while he is the pre-emptor, then there is no pre-emption for him.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage states another case where the right of pre-emption is voided. If the pre-emptor (shafi') guarantees the seller (ba'i') against the consequence of hidden defects (drk) in the property – that is, he agrees to indemnify the seller in case the buyer finds a defect and returns the property – then his right of pre-emption is lost. The reasoning is that by giving this guarantee, the pre-emptor has effectively consented to the sale and cannot later seek to nullify it by claiming pre-emption. This ruling is analogous to the previous cases where any action affirming the sale on the part of the pre-emptor removes his right. The Hanafi school considers this as a form of relinquishment (isqat) of the right. The text then begins another case ('and likewise if he sells with a condition of option for another') but the sentence is incomplete in the OCR.

Key Terms
ضمن الدرك (daman al-darak): Guarantee against hidden defects or eviction; an agreement by the pre-emptor to indemnify the seller if the property is found defective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the pre-emptor guarantees the seller against defects (daman al-darak), what happens to his right of pre-emption?
A) It remains intact.
B) It is void.
C) It is suspended until the guarantee ends.
D) It is transferred to the guarantor.

Key Takeaway
A pre-emptor who gives a guarantee against defects has accepted the sale and forfeits his right of pre-emption.

The General Principle of Shuf'a Entitlement
Establishes foundational entitlement rule for shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi principle of shuf'a entitlement and explain its rationale (ta'lil) by distinguishing between seller-associated and buyer-associated parties.

Core Idea
Shuf'a belongs to the buyer, never the seller, because shuf'a is a type of purchase — taking — which contradicts the seller's act of giving.

In the Book of Shuf'a, Al-Hidayah opens with a fundamental rule that determines who may claim pre-emption. This lesson unpacks that rule and the reasoning (ta'lil) behind it, which governs all subsequent cases. Understanding this principle is key to correctly applying shuf'a in various agency and mudaraba scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties
Parse the Arabic: 'من باع او بيع له' (whoever sells or for whom a sale is made) vs 'من اشترى او ابتيع له' (whoever buys or for whom a purchase is made). Note the passive forms covering agents and principals.
The principle covers both direct and indirect involvement.
2
State the ruling for each side
The seller side has no shuf'a; the buyer side has shuf'a. The Arabic says: 'لا شفعة له' for seller, 'فله الشفعة' for buyer.
This is a categorical ruling.
3
Understand the ta'lil (ratio)
The seller would be undoing his own sale (giving), while shuf'a is taking. The buyer's shuf'a is consistent with purchase (both taking). The author equates shuf'a with a purchase: 'الاخذ بالشفعة شراء'.
The rationale is based on logical consistency.
4
Apply to the four scenarios
Scenarios 1-2: agent/mudarib sells → principal/rabb al-mal associated with sale → no shuf'a. Scenarios 3-4: agent/mudarib buys → principal/rabb al-mal associated with purchase → shuf'a.
The association is key: who is on the seller side vs buyer side.
5
Generalize the principle
Shuf'a is a form of purchase; any party connected to the purchase side is entitled; any party connected to the sale side is not. This is the 'usul' for all shuf'a cases.
This principle overrides specific roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاصل ان من باع او بيع له لا شفعة له ومن اشترى او ابتيع له فله الشفعة لان الاول باخذ المشفوعة يسعى في نقض ما تم من جهته وهو البيع والمشترى لا ينقض شراه بالاخذ بالشفعة لانه مثل الشراء ترجمہ اور قاعدہ کلیہ یہ ہے کہ جس نے بچایا جس کیلئے بیچا گیا اسکے لئے شفعہ نہیں ہے اور جس نے خریدا یا جس کیلئے خریدا گیا اس کیلئے ہے اسلئے کہ اول مشفوعہ کے لینے کی وجہ سے اس چیز کے توڑنے میں سعی کر رہا ہے جو اس کی جانب سے نام ہوئی ہے اور وہ بیع ہے اور مشتری شفعہ میں لینے کی وجہ سے اپنی شراء کو نہیں توڑ رہا ہے اسلئے کہ یہ شفعہ میں لینا شراء کے مثل ہے۔ تشریح یہاں اولا یہ بات سمجھے کہ بیع و شراء کی یہاں چار صورتیں مذکور ہیں۔ (۱) بائع کا وکیل موکل کی طرف سے وکیل ہو کر فروخت کر دے۔ (۲) مضارب اس گھر کو فروخت کر دے جس کو اس نے مال مضاربت سے خریدا تھا اور رب المال اس کا شفیع ہے تو رب المال کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا۔ (۳) مشتری نے کسی مکان خریدنے کیلئے وکیل بنایا ہو۔ (۴) مضارب نے مال مضارب سے کوئی مال خریدا ہو اور رب المال اس کا شفیع ہو تو رب المال کوشفعہ لے گا۔ جب یہ چاروں صورتیں ذہن نشین ہو گئیں تو اب سمجھئے کہ صاحب ہدایہ فرمانا چاہتے ہیں کہ قانون کلی یہ ہے کہ پہلی دونوں صورتوں میں شفعہ نہیں ہے اور آخری دونوں صورتوں میں شفعہ ہے یعنی بیچ والی دونوں صورتوں میں شفعہ نہیں ہے اور شراء والی دونوں صورتوں میں شفعہ ہے، کیوں؟ اسلئے کہ اگر بیچ والی دونوں صورتوں میں شفعہ ملے تو یہ بات لازم آتی ہے کہ جو بیع کر رہا ہے یا جس کی وجہ سے بیج ہورہی ہے وہی اس کو توڑ رہا ہے کیونکہ بیع کا مال ہے دینا اور شفعہ مال ہے لینا، اور دینے اور لینے میں منافات ہے بیج اور شفعہ کے اندر منافات ہے۔ اور شراء کی دونوں صورتوں میں منافات نہیں ہے کیونکہ شراء کا مقصد بھی لینا ہے اور شفعہ کا مقصد بھی لینا ہے تو شراء اور شفعہ میں منافات نہیں۔ اسلئے یہ قانون کلی قرار پایا بالفاظ دیگر شفعہ میں لینا ایک قسم کی شراء ہے اور بیع وشراء میں منافات ہے تو بیع و شفعہ میں منافات ہوگی اور شراء اور شفعہ میں منافات نہ ہوگی۔
The principle is that whoever sells or for whom a sale is made has no shuf'a, and whoever buys or for whom a purchase is made has shuf'a, because the first person, by taking the pre-empted property, strives to undo what was completed from his side, which is the sale; and the buyer does not undo his purchase by taking through shuf'a, because it is like a purchase. Translation and the general rule is: whoever sells or for whom a sale is made—no shuf'a for him; and whoever buys or for whom a purchase is made—shuf'a is for him, because the first, by taking the pre-empted property, strives to undo what was completed from his side, which is the sale; and the buyer, by taking through shuf'a, does not undo his purchase, because taking through shuf'a is like a purchase. Explanation: First understand that there are four scenarios of sale and purchase here: (1) The seller's agent sells on behalf of the principal. (2) The mudarib sells the house he bought with mudaraba capital, and the rabb al-mal is his shafi'—the rabb al-mal does not get shuf'a. (3) The buyer appoints an agent to buy a house. (4) The mudarib buys something with mudaraba capital, and the rabb al-mal is his shafi'—the rabb al-mal gets shuf'a. Once these four scenarios are clear, understand that the author of Hidayah means that the general rule is: there is no shuf'a in the first two scenarios, and there is shuf'a in the last two scenarios—i.e., no shuf'a in the two sale scenarios, and shuf'a in the two purchase scenarios. Why? Because if shuf'a were given in the two sale scenarios, it would necessarily mean that the one who sold or due to whom the sale occurred is himself undoing it, because sale involves giving and shuf'a involves taking, and there is contradiction between giving and taking; there is contradiction between sale and shuf'a. In the two purchase scenarios there is no contradiction, because the purpose of purchase is also taking and the purpose of shuf'a is also taking, so there is no contradiction between purchase and shuf'a. Thus this general rule is established. In other words, taking through shuf'a is a type of purchase, and there is contradiction between sale and purchase, so there will be contradiction between sale and shuf'a, and no contradiction between purchase and shuf'a.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational ruling in the Book of Shuf'a: the general principle determining who is entitled to the right of pre-emption. The author first states the rule: the seller or the one on whose behalf a sale is made has no shuf'a; the buyer or the one on whose behalf a purchase is made has shuf'a. The reason (ta'lil) is that the seller is undoing his own sale, while the buyer's taking through shuf'a is equivalent to a purchase. The commentary then breaks down four scenarios: two involving sale (by agent or mudarib) and two involving purchase (by agent or mudarib). In the first two, the would-be shafi' is associated with the sale, so shuf'a is denied. In the last two, he is associated with the purchase, so shuf'a is granted. The underlying rationale is that sale (giving) and shuf'a (taking) are contradictory, whereas purchase (taking) and shuf'a (taking) are compatible. This is a key analytical tool for understanding all subsequent shuf'a rulings.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by paying its price, taking it from the buyer.
بائع (bāi‘): The seller; the person who executes the sale.
مشتري (mushtarī): The buyer; the person who purchases the property.
موكل (muwakkil): Principal who appoints an agent.
مضارب (mudārib): The working partner in a mudaraba contract.
رب المال (rabb al-māl): The capital provider in a mudaraba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general principle, who is entitled to the right of shuf'a?
A) The seller or one on whose behalf a sale is made
B) The buyer or one on whose behalf a purchase is made
C) Both the seller and the buyer
D) Neither the seller nor the buyer

Key Takeaway
The right of shuf'a belongs exclusively to the buyer side (anyone associated with the purchase) because shuf'a is a form of purchase, which is compatible with the act of buying, while it contradicts the seller's act of selling.

Exceptions to Shuf'a: Guarantee and Option Ratification
Details two exceptions where a shafi' loses his right due to his involvement in the sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state two specific cases where a shafi' loses his right of shuf'a and explain the underlying principle that distinguishes seller-side from buyer-side involvement.

Core Idea
A shafi' loses shuf'a if he is the one who completes the sale on the seller's side.

Shuf'a (pre-emption) generally allows a co-owner or neighbor to replace the buyer in a sale. However, if the shafi' himself plays a role that finalizes the sale, he cannot later claim the property through shuf'a, as that would contradict his own action. This lesson examines two specific scenarios where the shafi' loses his right: guaranteeing the seller's liability and ratifying an option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two exceptional cases
Read the passage and list the two situations where the shafi' loses shuf'a. First, when he guarantees the seller's daman al-darak. Second, when the seller stipulates an option for the shafi' and the shafi' ratifies it.
Daman al-darak is a guarantee to compensate the buyer for defects or eviction.
2
Understand the underlying principle
In both cases, the shafi' is directly involved in completing the sale: either by giving a guarantee that enables the buyer to proceed, or by ratifying an option that finalizes the contract. The shafi' cannot later undo what he has helped to complete.
The principle is that the shafi' must not be the cause of the sale's completion.
3
Apply the principle to distinguish seller-side vs. buyer-side
Compare the two cases. In the first two, the shafi' acts on behalf of the seller or ratifies the seller's contract. In the contrast case (where the buyer stipulates an option for the shafi' and the shafi' ratifies), the shafi' is on the buyer's side, completing the purchase. This does not conflict with shuf'a because both are acts of taking ownership.
The key distinction: involvement on the seller's side voids shuf'a; involvement on the buyer's side does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وکذالک لوضمن الدرك عن البائع وهو الشفيع فلا شفعة وكذالك اذا باع وشرط الخيار لغيره فامضى المشروط له الخيار البيع وهو الشفيع فلا شفعة له لان البيع تم بامضائه بخلاف جانب المشروط له الخيار من جانب المشترى. ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی اگر ضامن ہو گیا کوئی شخص بائع کی جانب سے تاوان و خسارہ کا اور وہی شفیع ہے تو اس کے لئے شفعہ نہیں ہے اور ایسے ہی جبکہ بائع نے بیچا اپنے غیر کیلئے اور خیار کی شرط کی ہو اور مشروطالہ الخیار نے بیع کو نافذ کر دیا اور وہی شفیع ہو تو اس کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا اسلئے کہ بیع اس کے نافذ کرنے کی وجہ سے نام ہوئی ہے۔ بخلاف مشروطالہ الخیار کی جانب کے مشتری کی جانب سے۔ تشریح مشتری کوئی مکان خریدنا چاہتا ہے مگر اس کو اس میں کچھ تردد ہے کہ شاید اس میں کوئی استحقاق کا دعوی کر دے یا کوئی اور تاوان و خسارہ کی صورت سامنے آجائے۔ مگر زید نے بائع کی جانب سے ضانت لی اور مشتری سے کہا کہ تو بے فکر ہوکر خرید لے اگر کوئی تاوان والی بات سامنے آئی تو میں اس کا ضامن ہوں کہ یہ مکان تمہارے حوالہ کردوں۔ یا بالفرض مکان دینے سے عاجز ہو جاؤں تو اس کی قیمت تمہارے حوالہ کر دوں اور اتفاق سے یہی زید اس گھر کا شفیع بھی ہے تو اب زید کوشفعہ نہیں ملے گا اس لئے کہ اس کی جانب سے بیج نام ہوئی تھی اور اس کی جانب سے اس کو توڑنا لازم آئے گا۔ ایک شخص نے اپنا مکان فروخت کیا مگر اس نے اپنے دوست زید کیلئے خیار کی شرط لگائی اور زید نے بیع کو نافذ کر دیا اتفاق سے یہی زید گھر کا شفیع بھی ہے تو اب زید کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا اسی دلیل سابق کی وجہ سے۔ ہاں اگر مشتری نے کوئی مکان خریدا اور اس نے زید کیلئے خیار شرط لگائی اور زید نے شراء کو نافذ کر دیا اور اتفاق سے یہی زید گھر کا شفیع بھی ہے تو اس صورت میں زید کو شفعہ ملے گا کیونکہ یہ شراء والا مسئلہ ہے اور ماقبل میں گذر چکا ہے کہ شراء اور شفعہ میں کچھ منافات نہیں کیونکہ دونوں کا مال لینا ہے۔
And likewise, if a person guarantees the daman al-darak (recompense for defects/eviction) on behalf of the seller, and he himself is the shafi', then he has no shuf'a. And likewise, when the seller sells and stipulates an option for someone else, and the person for whom the option is stipulated (the option-holder) ratifies the sale, and he himself is the shafi', then he has no shuf'a, because the sale was completed by his ratification, contrary to the case where the option-holder is on the side of the buyer. Translation: And likewise, if a person guarantees the compensation for loss on behalf of the seller and he is the shafi', then he has no shuf'a. And likewise, when the seller sells and stipulates an option for someone else, and the option-holder ratifies the sale and he is the shafi', then he gets no shuf'a, because the sale was completed by his ratification. Contrary to the case where the option-holder is on the side of the buyer. Explanation: A buyer wants to purchase a house but hesitates, fearing a claim of right or some loss. Then Zayd guarantees on behalf of the seller and tells the buyer to buy without worry; if any loss occurs, he will compensate. Zayd happens to be the shafi' of this house. Then Zayd does not get shuf'a, because the sale was completed from his side, and he would be undoing it. Similarly, if a man sells his house and stipulates an option for his friend Zayd, and Zayd ratifies the sale, and Zayd is also the shafi', then Zayd does not get shuf'a, due to the same reason. But if the buyer purchased a house and stipulated an option for Zayd, and Zayd ratified the purchase, and Zayd is the shafi', then in this case Zayd gets shuf'a, because this is a purchase scenario, and it has passed earlier that there is no contradiction between purchase and shuf'a, because both involve taking.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents two specific rulings that reinforce the general principle. First, if the shafi' guarantees the seller against any defect or eviction (daman al-darak), he loses his shuf'a. Second, if the seller stipulates an option (khiyar) for a third party who is the shafi', and that third party ratifies the sale, the shafi' loses his right. In both cases, the shafi' is effectively on the seller's side, ratifying or completing the sale, so he cannot claim the same right due to the conflict between sale and shuf'a. However, if the buyer stipulates the option for the shafi' and he ratifies the purchase, he retains shuf'a, because he is then on the buyer's side. The commentary provides practical examples: a buyer hesitant due to risks, and a seller setting a third-party option. The key is that the shafi' must not be the cause of completing the sale; otherwise, he would be undoing his own action.

Key Terms
ضمان الدرك (ḍamān al-darak): A guarantee given to the buyer by a third party to compensate for any defects, eviction, or loss that may arise from the sale, ensuring the buyer is indemnified.
خيار (khiyār): Option or right to rescind or confirm a contract within a specified period; the person for whom the option is stipulated has the right to ratify or reject the sale.
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption right of a co-owner or neighbor to replace a buyer in a sale of immovable property by paying the same price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller stipulates an option for the shafi' and the shafi' ratifies the sale, does the shafi' retain the right of shuf'a?
A) Yes, because the option was for the shafi'
B) No, because he completed the sale from the seller's side
C) Yes, as long as he did not also buy
D) No, only if he guarantees the seller

Key Takeaway
A shafi' loses his right of pre-emption if he guarantees the seller's liability or ratifies an option on the seller's side, as he cannot undo his own act that completed the sale.

Effect of Misinformation on Relinquishment of Shuf'a
Ruling when the shafi' relinquishes shuf'a based on incorrect price information.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a relinquishment of shuf'a based on a mistake about the price or type of consideration is void.

Core Idea
Relinquishment of shuf'a based on mistake is void — the right remains.

When a co-owner relinquishes his right of pre-emption (shuf'a) after hearing incorrect information about the sale, does he lose his right? This passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar explains that if the shafi' gives up his right based on a mistaken belief about the price or the commodity exchanged, the relinquishment is legally void. The Hanafi school bases this on the principle that consent given under a mistake is not valid consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is taslīm (relinquishment of shuf'a). The ruling is that it is bāṭil (void) when based on misinformation.
The ruling is explicit: 'his relinquishment is void and he has shuf'a.'
2
Locate the dalīl (proof)
The proof is a rational argument (ta'līl): the shafi' would not have relinquished had he known the truth; therefore his consent is defective.
The text states the reason: 'because he only relinquished due to considering the price too high in the first case, and due to the difficulty of the genus...'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The reasoning shows that the relinquishment is valid only if the shafi' has complete and accurate knowledge of the transaction. Mistake vitiates consent.
The legal cause (ʿilla) is the absence of valid consent (riḍā) due to ignorance of the true consideration.
4
Note the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies to all types of consideration: measurable (makīl), weighable (mawzūn), or countable items of similar type (ʿadadī mutaqārib).
The text explicitly extends the ruling: 'And similarly for every measurable or weighable or countable item of similar type.'
5
Identify the underlying principle
The maxim 'al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā' (matters are judged by intentions) applies: the intention behind the relinquishment matters. If the intention was based on a false premise, the act is invalid.
This principle is foundational in Hanafi fiqh for evaluating contracts and unilateral acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا بلغ الشفيع انها بيعت بالف درهم فسلم ثم علم انها بيعت باقل او بحنطة اوشعير قيمتها الف او اكثر فتسليمه باطل وله الشفعة لانه انما سلم لا ستكثار الثمن فى الاول ولتعذر الجنس الذي بلغه وتيسر ما بيع به في الثاني إذا الجنس مختلف وكذا كل مكيل او موزون او عددی متقارب ترجمہ امام قدوری نے فرمایا اور جبکہ شفیع کو یہ بر پہنچی کہ مکان ایک ہزار درہم میں بچا گیا ہے تو اس نے دست برداری دے دی۔ پھر اس کو معلوم ہوا کہ مکان کم میں بیچا گیا ہے یا گیہوں کے بدلے یا جو کے بدلے بیچا گیا ہے جس کی قیمت ہزار یا اس سے زیادہ ہے تو اس کی دست برداری باطل ہے اور اس کیلئے شفعہ ہے اس لئے کہ اس نے پہلی صورت میں شمن کو زیادہ سمجھنے کی وجہ سے دست برداری دی ہے اور دوسری صورت میں جنس کے متعد ر ہونے کی وجہ سے جس کی اس کو خبر پہنچی تھی اور اسکے آسان ہونے کی وجہ سے جس کے ذریعہ بیچ ہوئی ہے اسلئے کہ جنس مختلف ہے اور ایسے ہی ہر مکیلی یا موزونی یا عددی متقارب۔
He (al-Quduri) said: And if the shafi' is informed that it (the property) was sold for one thousand dirhams, so he relinquishes (shuf'a), then he learns that it was sold for less, or for wheat or barley whose value is one thousand or more, then his relinquishment is void and he has shuf'a, because he only relinquished due to considering the price too high in the first case, and due to the difficulty of the genus (type) he was informed of and the ease of what it was sold with in the second case, since the genus differs. And similarly for every measurable or weighable or countable item of similar type. Translation: Imam Quduri said: And when the shafi' is informed that the house was sold for a thousand dirhams, so he relinquishes, then he learns that it was sold for less, or for wheat or barley whose value is a thousand or more, then his relinquishment is void and he has shuf'a, because he only relinquished due to considering the price excessive in the first case, and due to the difficulty of the genus he was informed of and the ease of what it was sold with in the second case, because the genus differs. And similarly for every measurable, weighable, or countable item of similar type.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where the shafi' relinquishes his right based on false or incomplete information about the sale price. Two cases: (1) He hears the price is 1000 dirhams, so he gives up, then learns the actual price is lower. (2) He hears the price is in a difficult-to-acquire commodity (e.g., wheat), then learns the actual transaction was for a different commodity (e.g., barley) that is easier to obtain, or worth the same or more. In both cases, his relinquishment is void because it was based on a misapprehension; he would not have relinquished if he knew the truth. The ruling extends to all measurable (makil), weighable (mawzun), or countable (adadi) items of similar type. The underlying reason is that the relinquishment was due to a mistake about the price or the type of consideration, so it does not reflect a valid intention to abandon the right.

Key Terms
تسليم (taslīm): Relinquishment or abandonment of the right of shuf'a by the shafi' after learning of the sale.
مكيل (makīl): Items sold by measure (e.g., grains).
موزون (mawzūn): Items sold by weight (e.g., metals, liquids).
عددي متقارب (adadi mutaqārib): Items sold by count that are similar in value and type (e.g., eggs, nuts).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a shafi' relinquishes his right after hearing the property was sold for 1000 dirhams, but later learns it was sold for 800 dirhams, what is the ruling?
A) His relinquishment is valid and he cannot claim shuf'a
B) His relinquishment is void and he still has shuf'a
C) He must pay the difference to regain shuf'a
D) He loses the right only if the price difference is significant

Key Takeaway
Relinquishment of shuf'a based on a mistake about the price or type of consideration is void, and the shafi' retains the right of pre-emption.
