قال وتسليم الاب والوصى الشفعة على الصغير جائز عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف وقال محمد وزفر رحمهم الله هو على شفعته اذا بلغ قالوا وعلى هذا الخلاف اذا بلغهما شراء دار بجوار دار الصبى فلم يطلبا الشفعة وعلى هذا الخلاف تسليم الوكيل بطلب الشفعة في رواية كتاب الوكالة وهو الصحيح

ترجمہ (ایام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں ) فرمایا اور باپ اور وصی کا سپر د کر دینا شفعہ کو بچہ کے اوپر جائز ہے ابوحنیفہ اورام بو یوسف کے نزدیک اور فرمایا محمد اول از فقر نے کہ بچہ اپنے شفعہ پر رہے گا جبکہ بالغ ہو جائے۔ مشائخ نے فرمایا ہے اور اس اختلاف پر ہے جبکہ ان دونوں کو بچہ کے گھر کے پڑوس میں گھر کی خرید کی خبر پہنچی ہو پس انہوں نے شفعہ طلب نہ کیا ہو اور اسی اختلاف پر ہے طلب شفعہ کے وکیل کا دست برداری دینا مبسوط کی کتاب الوکالۃہ کے مطابق اور یہی صحیح ہے۔

تشریح صغیر کی ماں کا انتقال ہو گیا تھا جس سے اس کو ایک مکان میراث میں مل تھا اب اس کے پڑوس میں ایک مکان فروخت ہوا جس میں بچہ کو شفعہ پہنچتا ہے مگر باپ نے یا باپ نہ ہونے کی صورت میں وصی نے شفعہ سے دست برداری دے دی تو یہ صحیح ہے یا نہیں۔ تو حضرات شیخین نے اس کو جائز کہاور امام محمد و زفر نے اس کو جائز نہیں کہا بلکہ اس ک معتبر نہیں مانا۔ اور بچہ بالغ ہونے کے بعد اپنے شفعہ پر برقرار رہے گا۔ ان دونوں نے صراحت دست برداری دے دی ہو یا شفعہ کی طلب نہ کی ہو دونوں صورتوں میں یہی اختلاف ہے۔ اور اگر کسی نے کسی کو اپنے لئے طلب شفعہ کا وکیل بنایا ہو اس گھر کے اندر جو اس کے پڑوس میں فروخت ہوا اور اس وکیل نے شف۔ طلب کے بجائے شفعہ سے دست برداری دے دی ہو تو اس میں بھی یہی اختلاف ہے۔

اس کو مصنف نے فرمایا: وهو الصحیح، یہ اسلئے فرمایا تا کہ اس روایت کی تردید ہو جائے جس میں حضرات طرفین کو ایک ساتھہ کہا گیا ہے اور امام ابو یوسف کے قول کو ان دونوں کے خلاف کہا گیا ہے۔

امام محمد و امام زفر کی دلیل .

لمحمد وزفرانه حق ثابت للصغير فلا يملكان ابطاله كدينه وقوده ولانه شرع لدفع الضرر فكان ابطاله إضرارا به

ترجمہ اما محمد اد از فرکی دلیل یہ ہے کہ حق شفعہ ایسا حق ہے جو بچہ کیلئے ثابت ہے تو یہ دونوں اس کے ابطال کے مالک نہ ہوں گے جیسا کہ اس کی دیت اور اس کا قصاص اور اس لئے کہ یہ حق مشروع ہوا ہے ضرر کو دور کرنے کیلئے تو اس کا ابطال بچہ کو نقصان پہنچانا ہو جائے گا۔ تشریح یہ اما محمد اور امام زفرتی دلیل ہے کہ جیسے باپ اور وصی بچہ کی دیت اور اس کے قصاص کو معاف نہیں کر سکتے کیونکہ وہ منجانب شرع حق ثابت ہے اسی طرح حق شفعہ کو بھی باطل نہیں کر سکتے اور مشروعیت شفعہ کا مقصد دفع ضرر ہے تو اگر اس کا حق باطل کر دیا گیا تو بجائے دفع شر کے بچہ کیلئے اضرار اور نقصان دینا ہے لہذا یہ دونوں ابطال کے مالک نہ ہوں گے۔

شیخین کی دلیل

ولهما انه في معنى التجارة فيملكان تركه الاترى ان من اوجب بيعا للصبي صح رده من الأب والوصى ولانه دائر بين النفع والضرر وقد يكون النظر فى تركه ليبقى الثمن على ملكه والولاية نظرية فيملكانه وسكوتهـما كـابـطـالهـمـا لكونه دليل ألاعراض هذا اذا بيعت بمثل قيمتها

ترجمہ اور شیخین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ ( شفعہ میں لینا) تجارت کے درجہ میں ہے تو وہ دونوں اس لئے ترک کے مالک ہوں گے کیا آپ دیکھتے نہیں کہ جس نے بچہ کیلئے بیج کا ایجاب کیا تو باپ اور وصی کی طرف سے اس ایجاب کو رد کرنا صحیح ہے اور اس لئے کہ یہ نفع اور نقصان کے درمیان دائر ہے اور کبھی شفقت اس کے چھوڑ دینے میں ہوتی ہے تا کہ ثمن بچہ کی ملکیت پر باقی رہے اور ولایت نظری ہے تو وہ دونوں اس کے مالک ہوں گے اور ان دونوں کا سکوت ان کے ابطال کے مثل ہے اس کی دلیل اعراض ہونے کی وجہ سے اور یہ اختلاف اس وقت ہے جبکہ گھر کو اس کی قیمت کے مثل کے بدلہ فروخت کیا جائے۔
The relinquishment of shuf'ah by the wasi: will the minor's right to shuf'ah remain or not? Statements of the jurists.

He said: The father and wasi's relinquishment of shuf'ah on behalf of the minor is permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad and Zufar said: He remains on his shuf'ah when he reaches maturity. They said: And upon this disagreement is the case when both of them are informed of the purchase of a house adjacent to the minor's house and they do not demand shuf'ah. And upon this disagreement is the relinquishment by the agent for demanding shuf'ah in the narration of Kitab al-Wakalah, and it is the correct view.

Translation: (Imam Muhammad said in Jami' al-Saghir) The father and wasi's surrender of shuf'ah on the minor is permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad and Zufar said: The minor remains on his shuf'ah when he becomes major. The mashayikh said: And upon this disagreement is when they both are informed of the purchase of a house adjacent to the minor's house and they do not demand shuf'ah. And upon this disagreement is the relinquishment by the agent for demanding shuf'ah according to the book of agency, and this is correct.

Explanation: The minor's mother died, from whom he inherited a house. Now a house adjacent to it is sold, and the minor has shuf'ah. But the father, or in his absence the wasi, relinquishes shuf'ah. Is this valid? The two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) said it is permissible; Imam Muhammad and Zufar said it is not permissible and they do not consider it valid. The minor will remain on his shuf'ah after maturity. Whether they explicitly relinquish or do not demand shuf'ah, the same disagreement applies. And if someone appoints an agent to demand shuf'ah for him in a house sold adjacent to his, and the agent relinquishes shuf'ah instead of demanding it, the same disagreement applies.

The author said: 'And it is the correct view' — he said this to refute the narration in which both parties are said to agree, and the statement of Abu Yusuf is said to be against both of them.

The proof of Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar:

For Muhammad and Zufar, it is a right established for the minor, so they do not have the authority to invalidate it, like his blood-money and his retaliation, and because it was legislated to repel harm, so invalidating it would be causing harm to him.

Translation: The proof of Imam Muhammad and Zufar is that the right of shuf'ah is a right established for the minor, so these two (father and wasi) do not have the authority to invalidate it, just like his diyah (blood-money) and his qisas (retaliation). And because this right was legislated to repel harm, so its invalidation would be causing harm to him. Explanation: This is the proof of Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar — just as the father and wasi cannot forgive the minor's diyah and qisas because they are established rights from the Shari'ah, similarly they cannot invalidate the right of shuf'ah. And the purpose of legislation of shuf'ah is to repel harm, so if his right is invalidated, instead of repelling harm, it brings harm and loss to the minor. Hence they do not have authority to invalidate it.

The proof of the two Shaykhs:

And for them, it (taking by shuf'ah) is in the sense of trade, so they have the authority to leave it. Do you not see that if someone gives an offer of sale for the minor, the rejection of that offer by the father or wasi is valid? And because it oscillates between benefit and harm, and sometimes consideration (maslahah) lies in leaving it so that the price remains in the minor's ownership. And guardianship is based on consideration (nazari), so they have authority over it. And their silence is like their invalidation because it is evidence of turning away. This is when the house is sold for its equivalent value.

Translation: And the proof of the two Shaykhs is that this (taking by shuf'ah) is in the nature of trade, so they have authority to leave it. Do you not see that if someone makes an offer of sale to the minor, the rejection of that offer by the father or wasi is valid? And because it oscillates between benefit and harm, and sometimes consideration lies in leaving it so that the price remains in the minor's ownership. And guardianship is based on consideration, so they have authority over it. And their silence is like their invalidation because it is evidence of abandonment. This is when the house is sold for its equivalent value.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a father or wasi (executor) can validly relinquish the right of shuf'ah (preemption) on behalf of a minor child. The author first cites the matn from Imam Muhammad's Jami' al-Saghir: According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Shaykhayn), it is permissible; according to Muhammad and Zufar, the minor retains the right upon reaching maturity. The disagreement extends to cases where the guardian fails to demand shuf'ah after being informed, or when an agent appointed to demand shuf'ah instead relinquishes it. The author notes that the correct view is that of the Shaykhayn, as stated in Kitab al-Wakalah. The explanation (tashreeh) provides a concrete scenario: the minor inherits a house, a neighboring house is sold, and the father or wasi gives up the shuf'ah. The Shaykhayn allow it; Muhammad and Zufar do not. The author clarifies that explicit relinquishment or mere silence (not claiming) both fall under the same disagreement. The passage then presents the proofs. Muhammad and Zufar argue that shuf'ah is a firmly established right for the minor, like diyah (blood-money) and qisas (retaliation), which the guardian cannot forfeit. Invalidating it would harm the minor, contradicting the purpose of shuf'ah (repelling harm). The Shaykhayn counter that shuf'ah is like trade (tijarah); guardians have discretionary authority (wilaya nazariyya) over the minor's financial matters, as they can reject an offer of sale. Since shuf'ah may bring benefit or harm, the guardian may decide to leave it to preserve the minor's wealth. Silence (not claiming) is akin to relinquishment. The dispute is conditioned on the house being sold at fair value; otherwise, different rules may apply.

Key Terms
وصی (wasi): The executor or guardian appointed by the father to manage the minor's affairs after the father's death.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of preemption entitling a neighbor or co-owner to acquire sold property by paying its price.
ولایت نظری (wilaya nazariyya): Guardianship based on consideration (nazar) i.e., the guardian must act in the best interest of the minor, and has discretion accordingly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in this passage, can a father validly give up the shuf'ah right of his minor child?
A) No, because shuf'ah is a fixed right like diyah and cannot be waived.
B) Yes, because the father has discretionary authority over the minor's financial affairs.
C) Only if the minor consents after reaching maturity.
D) Only if the house is sold below market value.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a guardian (father or wasi) may validly relinquish a minor's right of preemption (shuf'ah) because it is treated like a trade transaction, provided the sale is at fair value.

Father and Executor's Waiver of Shuf'ah for Minor in Cases of Ghubn Fahish
Expanded commentary on an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding shuf'ah waiver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule on the validity of a father or executor's waiver of shuf'ah for a minor in cases of excessive price deviation, and identify the relied-upon position within the school.

Core Idea
A waiver of shuf'ah is invalid when the guardian lacks the right to take, as in cases of ghubn fahish.

When a property is sold far above or below its market value, the father or executor may consider waiving the minor's right of pre-emption. However, the Hanafi school distinguishes between ordinary sales and those with excessive price deviation (ghubn fahish). In this lesson, we examine the intra-Hanafi dispute and the principle that waiver is a branch of the right to take.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two cases: sale at a price far above market value with ghubn fahish, and sale at a price far below market value with excessive concession (muhabah). For each, the question is whether the father/executor's waiver of shuf'ah is valid.
2
State the two opinions
For the above-market case, there are two views: (1) waiver is valid by consensus because it is purely beneficial; (2) waiver is invalid by agreement because the guardian does not have the right to take. The second view is considered more correct (asbah).
For the below-market case, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the waiver invalid; no report from Abu Yusuf.
3
Trace the principle
The key principle is that waiver (taslim) is a branch (far') of taking (akhdh). Only one who has the right to take shuf'ah can validly waive it. In ghubn fahish, the guardian lacks the right to take because taking would harm the minor.
The opposite view argues that waiver is purely beneficial and does not require the right to take.
4
Apply qiyas
The commentator compares the guardian in ghubn fahish to a stranger (ajnabi). A stranger has no right to take, so his waiver is invalid. Likewise, the guardian cannot waive what he cannot take.
This qiyas strengthens the second view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the waiver is invalid in both above- and below-market cases of ghubn fahish. The child retains the right to pre-empt the property from the buyer.
Tarjih authority: the commentator states the second view is asbah (most correct).

Book of Shuf'ah. Explanation: The proof of the two shaykhs, the gist of which is that shuf'ah is a type of transaction which involves potential profit and loss, and guardianship over a child is granted so that kindness is shown to him. Sometimes kindness may be in abandoning shuf'ah because that is what expediency demands. Therefore, the father and executor acquired this right that they may leave taking shuf'ah. Just as the explicit annulment has the same ruling, silence also has the same ruling because silence is evidence of disinterest, so in both cases disinterest is found: in the first explicitly and in the second by indication. But this difference of theirs is when the house is sold at its fair value. If it is sold with increase or decrease, its ruling will come later. If the house is sold with ghubn fahish, then the executor waives shuf'ah – is this waiver valid or not? Statements of jurists. 'If it is sold for more than its value by an amount such that people do not overlook such a loss, then it was said: the waiver is valid by consensus because it is purely beneficial; and it was said: it is not valid by agreement because he does not have the right to take, so he does not have the right to waive, like a stranger. And if it is sold for less than its value with much concession, then from Abu Hanifa: the waiver from both is not valid, and no narration from Abu Yusuf. And Allah knows best.' Translation: So if the house is sold for more than its value by such an increase that people do not bear that amount of loss, then it was said: by consensus the waiver is valid because it is purely kindness; and it was said: by agreement the waiver is not valid, because neither of them has the right to take shuf'ah, so they will not have the right to waive, like a stranger. And if it is sold for much less than its value with concession, then from Abu Hanifa it is narrated that the waiver from both is not valid, and from Abu Yusuf there is no narration. And Allah knows best. Explanation: If the value of the house is, for example, three thousand and it is sold for five thousand, then such a loss is not customary for people to bear, and if one of them gave a waiver, what is the ruling? The author says there are two statements: First statement: by consensus the waiver is valid because it is purely kindness. Second statement, which is considered most correct: the waiver is not valid; rather the child's right remains as before. 'Oh, what strange logic?' Answer: Consider that waiver is a derivative of taking, i.e., only the person who has the right to take shuf'ah can give a waiver. And here, due to ghubn fahish, the father and executor do not have the right to take shuf'ah. So now they become like a stranger. Just as the waiver of a stranger is not valid, likewise the waiver of these two is not valid. And if the value of the house is, for example, five thousand and due to concession and leniency the seller sold it for three thousand, then what is the ruling? Imam Abu Hanifa says: the waiver of father and executor will not be valid because there is clear loss for the child. What does Imam Abu Yusuf say? Answer: There is no narration from him on this matter. And Allah knows best. Alert: It is in Majma' al-Anhur, vol. 2, p. 469, and in al-Kafi: When the father waives the shuf'ah of a minor and the purchase is for much less than its value, from Imam (Abu Hanifa) that the waiver is allowed because it is refraining from bringing the property into his ownership, not removing from his ownership, and it is not a gift; and from Muhammad that it is not allowed because it is tantamount to a gift of his property; and there is no narration from Abu Yusuf. And in al-Tabyin there is discussion, so consult.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the Hidayat dealing with an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a father or executor (wasi) waiving the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) on behalf of a minor child when the property is sold at a price significantly above or below its market value. The author begins by stating the general principle: shuf'ah is a transaction that involves profit and loss, and guardianship over a minor is given for the purpose of kindness (shafaqah). Therefore, the father and executor have the right to waive shuf'ah if it is in the child's interest. This waiver can be explicit or by silence (sukut), as silence indicates disinterest (i'rad). However, this applies only when the property is sold at its fair value. The present discussion concerns cases of ghubn fahish (excessive deception in price). The commentator then quotes the Arabic matn: if the house is sold for more than its value by an amount that people do not overlook (la yataghaban al-nas bihi), there are two opinions: one says the waiver is valid by consensus (ijma') because it is purely beneficial; the other says it is not valid by agreement because the father/executor does not have the right to take shuf'ah, so they cannot waive it — just like a stranger (ajnabi). The second opinion is considered more correct (asbah). The reasoning is that waiver is a branch of taking: only one who has the right to take can waive. Since ghubn fahish removes the right to take (because taking would harm the child), the waiver is also invalid. Thus the father/executor is like a stranger. The passage then addresses the case where the house is sold for much less than its value with great concession (muhabah). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the waiver is not valid because it constitutes a clear loss to the child. No narration is reported from Abu Yusuf. The commentator adds an important alert (tanbih) from Majma' al-Anhur and al-Kafi, where a different narration from Abu Hanifa (Imam) is cited: the waiver is allowed because it is refraining from bringing the property into ownership, not a gift; but Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it is not allowed because it is like a gift of the child's property. The commentator notes that there is no narration from Abu Yusuf on this matter and points to al-Tabyin for further discussion. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the text appears to be that the waiver is not valid in cases of ghubn fahish, whether the sale is above or below market value.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption in property sales, allowing a co-owner or neighbour to purchase the property from the buyer
غبن فاحش (ghubn fahish): Excessive deception in price where the difference is so large that people would not ordinarily tolerate it
تسليم (taslim): Waiver or relinquishment of the right of shuf'ah
محاباة (muhabah): Concession or favor in price, selling below market value

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a property is sold at a price significantly below its market value (ghubn fahish with muhabah), what is the ruling on the father or executor's waiver of shuf'ah on behalf of a minor child according to the relied-upon view in this passage?
A) The waiver is valid because it is purely beneficial
B) The waiver is invalid because they are like strangers and cannot waive
C) The waiver is valid only if the buyer is a relative
D) The waiver is valid only if the executor is the father

Key Takeaway
In cases of ghubn fahish, a father or executor cannot validly waive a minor's shuf'ah because they lack the right to take; the child's right remains intact.

Compulsory Division of Livestock: Same vs Different Species
Ruling on compulsory division of livestock by species

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a Qadi can compel division of jointly owned livestock and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on species uniformity and analogy with debt repayment.

Core Idea
The Qadi can compel division only when livestock are of the same species—equality is achievable and compulsion is analogous to debt recovery.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies a practical issue in partnership (shirkah) and division (qismah) of livestock. It distinguishes between joint ownership of animals of the same species versus different species and explains when a Qadi can force division at the request of one partner. The ruling is based on the principle that compulsion is permissible in exchanges where equality can be approximated, drawing an analogy with the compulsion exercised in recovering debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between two cases: livestock of the same species (e.g., all goats) and livestock of different species (e.g., goats, buffaloes, horses). Note the ruling for each.
2
Understand the condition for compulsion
For same-species animals, if one partner requests division and the other refuses, the Qadi can compel division. The key is that the animals are homogenous in value and purpose, making equality feasible.
Compulsion (jabr) is allowed only when the property is of one genus.
3
Trace the qiyas (analogy) with debt repayment
The text compares compulsion in division to compulsion in debt recovery: a Qadi can arrest a debtor to force payment. Similarly, in exchange (mubadalah) of shares, compulsion is permissible because the partner is seeking his right to exclusive benefit.
The analogy establishes that compulsory exchange is valid when value can be estimated.
4
Apply the rule to different species
If the animals are of different species, compulsion is not applied because gross disparity (tafawut fahish) makes equality difficult. However, if all partners voluntarily agree, division is permissible.
Voluntary agreement overrides the prohibition of compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لیکن اس میں دیکھا جائے کہ مبادلہ کی صورت میں حیوانات وغیرہ ایک جنس کے ہیں یا مختلف جنس کے۔ ایک جنس کے مثلا بکریاں یا بیل ہیں اور مختلف الاجناس کہ کچھ بکریاں ہوں اور کچھ بھینس ہو اور کچھ گھوڑے ہوں ۔
پھر ان دونوں صورتوں میں قسمت کے اندر کچھ فرق ہو گا۔ یعنی پہلی صورت میں اگر شرکاء میں سے کسی نے بھی قاضی سے ہٹوارہ کی درخواست کی اور دوسرے شرکاء بنوارہ پر رضامند نہیں ہیں تو قاضی ان کو بٹوارہ پر رضامند کرے گا اور بنوارہ کر دے گا۔ کیونکہ یہاں متحد الجنس میں ان جانوروں کا مقصد متقارب ہے تو اس میں افراز کے معنی پائے جاتے ہیں اور مبادلہ تو یہ ہے ہی۔
بہر حال یہاں قاضی جبر کرے گا کیونکہ ایسے مبادلہ میں جبر جاری ہوتا ہے۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ قضاء دین میں جبر جاری ہوتا ہے حالانکہ دین میں مقبوض عین دین نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اس کا بدل ہوتا ہے اور اگر عین دین کو واجب کیا جائے تو قرض لینے کا فائدہ فوت ہو جائے گا تو قاضی مدیون بگو گر فتار کر کے قید خانہ میں ڈال سکتا ہے۔ یہاں تک وہ اپنا مال و اسباب فروخت کر کے قرض ادا کرے۔
تو اس تقریر سے معلوم ہوا کہ مبادلہ کے اندر جبر جاری ہوتا ہے۔ یہاں ایک شریک کی درخواست پر قاضی جبر کیوں کرے گا؟ تو اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ وہ شریک قاضی سے بھی درخواست کرتا ہے کہ مجھ کو میری ملک سے انتفاع کا خاص حق دیا جائے اور دوسروں کو میری ملک سے فائدہ اٹھانے سے روک دیا جائے۔ تو اس درخواست میں وہ حق بجانب ہے تو قاضی پر ضروری ہوا کہ اس کی درخواست قبول کرے اور بٹوارہ کرے۔
اور اگر دوسری صورت ہو کہ وہ مختلف اجناس ہوں تو پھر قاضی دوسرے شرکاء کو بٹوارہ پر مجبور نہیں کرے گا۔ کیونکہ ان کے درمیان تفاوت فاحش ہونے کی وجہ سے مساوات و برابری مشکل ہے۔
ہاں اگر وہ اس صورت میں بھی سب بینوارہ پر راضی ہو جائیں تو چونکہ حق تو انہیں کا ہے تو بٹوارہ جائز ہے۔
But in this it should be seen whether in the case of exchange the animals etc. are of one species or of different species. Of one species, for example, goats or oxen; and of different species, such that some are goats, some buffaloes, and some horses. Then in both these cases there will be some difference in division. That is, in the first case, if any of the partners requests the Qadi for division and the other partners are not satisfied with division, then the Qadi will make them agree to division and will divide. Because here, in the case of uniform species, the purpose of these animals is similar, so the meaning of separation (ifraz) is found in it, and it is indeed an exchange. In any case, the Qadi will compel because compulsion is applied in such exchange. This is why compulsion is applied in repayment of debt, even though in debt the possessed object is not the specific debt (ayn al-dayn) but its substitute; and if the specific debt were made obligatory, the benefit of borrowing would be lost. So the Qadi can arrest the debtor and put him in prison until he sells his goods and pays the debt. So from this discussion it is understood that compulsion is applied in exchange. Why would the Qadi compel at the request of one partner? The reason is that that partner requests the Qadi to give him the special right of benefit from his property and to prevent others from benefiting from his property. In this request he is justified, so it becomes necessary for the Qadi to accept his request and make the division. And if the second case occurs, that they are of different species, then the Qadi will not compel the other partners to divide, because due to the gross disparity between them equality is difficult. Yes, if they all become satisfied with division even in this case, then since the right is theirs, division is permissible.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling on compulsory division (qismah jabriyyah) of livestock among co-owners. The key distinction is whether the animals are of the same genus or different genera. If they are of the same species (e.g., all goats), the Qadi can compel division because the animals are similar in value and purpose, making the division effectively a separation (ifraz) rather than an exchange. Compulsion is allowed here by analogy (qiyas) to the compulsion in repayment of debts, where the debtor is forced to sell assets. If the species differ, compulsion is not applied due to the difficulty of maintaining equality. However, if all partners voluntarily agree, division is valid even for different species. This ruling reflects the Hanafi principle that compulsory exchange (mubadalah) is permitted when the goods are homogenous and value can be approximated.

Key Terms
قسمت (qismah): Division of jointly owned property among co-owners.
جبر (jabr): Legal compulsion by Qadi in certain types of division.
مبادلہ (mubadalah): Exchange where each co-owner receives a portion in lieu of their share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can a Qadi compel division of jointly owned livestock?
A) Only when all partners agree.
B) When the animals are of different species, because the Qadi must ensure equality.
C) When the animals are of the same species and one partner requests division.
D) In all cases of joint ownership because the Qadi has authority to end co-ownership.

Key Takeaway
The Qadi's power to compel division of jointly owned livestock is limited to cases where the animals are of the same species, because equality is achievable and the partner has a right to exclusive benefit.

Distribution of the Qasim's Fee: Per Capita or Proportional?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sharing of division fee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on distributing the division fee, explain the reasoning (tamayyuz vs mu'nat al-milk), and apply the ruling to a given co-ownership scenario.

Core Idea
The qasim's fee is divided equally per capita because it compensates the uniform act of separation, not the value of shares.

When co-owners with unequal shares hire a divider, how should his fee be split? Abu Hanifa argues the fee is for the act of separating shares—an effort that does not vary by share size. The Sahibayn treat it as a maintenance cost, proportional to ownership. This lesson unpacks their proofs and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the legal question: how should the qasim's fee be divided among co-owners with unequal shares?
2
Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
State his view (equal per capita) and his proof: the fee is compensation for tamayyuz (separation), which is uniform; proportionality is impractical due to difficulty and possible reverse calculations.
3
Sahibayn's position and dalil
State their view (proportional to shares) and their proof: the fee is a maintenance cost (mu'nat al-milk), analogized to the hire of a weighman, digging a well, and maintaining a shared slave.
4
Abu Hanifa's rebuttal
Analyze how Abu Hanifa distinguishes the analogies: in digging a well, the fee varies with earth moved; in measuring/weighing not for division, the fee varies with work. For division itself, the act of separation is the same for each share.
5
Thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Determine the practical difference: e.g., shares 1/2, 1/3, 1/6, fee 12. Abu Hanifa: each pays 4; Sahibayn: 6, 4, 2. Note that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's per capita view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القسمة
قال واجرة القسمة على عدد الرؤس عند ابي حنيفة قال ابو يوسف ومحمد على قدر الانصباء لانه مؤنة الملك فيتقدر بقدره كاجرة الكيال والوزان وحضر البير المشتركة ونفقة المملوك المشترك
ولابى حنيفة ان الاجر مقابل بالتمييز وانه لا يتفاوت وربما يصعب الحساب بالنظر الى القليل وقد ينعكس الامر فتعذر اعتباره فيتعلق الحكم باصل التمييز بخلاف حفر البير لان الاجر مقابل ينقل التراب وهو يتفاوت
والكيل والوزن ان كان للقسمة قيل هو على الخلاف وان لم يكن القسمة فالأجر مقابل بعمل الكيل والوزن وهو يتفاوت
The Book of Division. He said: The fee for division is according to the number of persons (heads) according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: according to the value of the shares, because it is a burden of ownership, so it is measured by its extent, like the fee of the weighman and the measure, and the digging of a shared well, and the maintenance of a shared slave. And for Abu Hanifa: the fee is in exchange for separation (tamayyuz), and it does not vary; sometimes the calculation becomes difficult when looking at the small share, and sometimes the opposite occurs, so considering it becomes impossible, so the ruling is attached to the essence of separation, unlike digging a well because the fee is in exchange for moving earth, and it varies. And as for measuring and weighing: if it is for division, it is said it is subject to the same difference; if it is not for division, then the fee is in exchange for the act of measuring and weighing, and it varies.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding how the fee of the qasim (the divider) should be distributed among co-owners. The dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The issue arises when co-owners with unequal shares hire a divider. The core question: should the fee be divided equally per capita or proportionally according to each owner's share? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the fee is divided equally among all co-owners regardless of their share size. His reasoning: the fee is compensation for the act of separating (tamayyuz) each owner's share from the common property. This act of separation is essentially the same for each share, whether large or small. The effort involved does not differ significantly, and attempting to calculate proportionally would be cumbersome and often impractical. Therefore, the fee is tied to the act of separation itself, which is uniform. The Sahibayn argue that the fee is a maintenance cost (mu'nat al-milk) of the property, and thus should be borne in proportion to each owner's share of ownership. They analogize to other joint expenses: the fee for a weighman or measure hired by co-owners to weigh or measure their shared goods, the cost of digging a shared well, and the maintenance of a jointly owned slave. In all these cases, the expense is divided according to ownership shares. The text then presents Abu Hanifa's rebuttal to the analogy of digging a well: in digging a well, the fee is directly proportional to the amount of earth moved, which varies with the depth or portion each owner requires. Since the work is quantifiably different, the fee naturally varies. But in division, the work of separating shares is essentially the same for each share, regardless of size. Finally, the text addresses the Sahibayn's analogy to measuring and weighing. It distinguishes between measuring/weighing for the purpose of division and measuring/weighing for other purposes. If the measurement is itself part of the division process, then the same dispute applies (Abu Hanifa would consider it equal per head, while the Sahibayn would apply proportional shares). If the measurement is done for a different purpose and is not part of the division, then the fee is in exchange for the specific act of measuring/weighing, which varies with the quantity, and thus the fee follows the proportion of work. This nuance defends Abu Hanifa's position by showing that the Sahibayn's analogy is not parallel when the context of division is properly considered. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here, but the commentary notes that Imam al-Sarkhasi in al-Mabsut favored Abu Hanifa's view because the fee is contractually tied to the act of separation, not to the quantum of ownership.

Key Terms
أجرة القسمة (ujrat al-qismah): The fee paid to the divider (qasim) for partitioning jointly owned property.
عدد الرؤوس (adad al-ru'us): The number of persons (co-owners) regardless of their share sizes.
تمييز (tamayyuz): The act of separating each owner's share from the common property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how is the qasim's fee distributed among co-owners?
A) Proportionally to each owner's share
B) Equally per capita regardless of share size
C) According to the amount of work done for each owner
D) The fee is paid only by the owner with the largest share

Key Takeaway
The division fee is compensated for the uniform act of separation, so the Hanafi mufta bihi divides it equally per capita, not by share size.

Division Fee: Equality over Proportionality
Explaining Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on equal division fee and refuting Sahibayn's analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the division fee, explain the reasoning behind equality versus proportionality, and apply the ruling to a partner distribution scenario.

Core Idea
In property division, the fee is for distinguishing shares, which is equal effort — so it is shared equally.

When partners divide joint property, who pays the measurer? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students differed on this. The dispute hinges on whether the service is uniform or varies with share size. Understanding this reveals how the Hanafi school refines analogical reasoning (qiyas).

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Identify the two positions: Imam Abu Hanifa says the fee is equal among partners; Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is proportional to each partner's share.
The fee is for the act of distinguishing (tamyiz) shares.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's proof
The fee compensates for the act of distinguishing, which is uniform regardless of share size. The effort does not increase with a larger share.
Compare to counting heads: each head requires equal effort.
3
Identify Sahibayn's analogy
Sahibayn analogize to digging a well (fee varies with earth moved) and to weighing/measuring for sale (effort increases with quantity).
They argue that fee should correspond to benefit or effort.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's refutation
Well-digging involves moving soil, which varies; but division is about distinguishing, not moving. For measuring/weighing, if for division, the same dispute applies; if for quantity determination only, fee is proportional but that is a different service.
The key distinction: the contractual object (معقود عليه) in division is tamyiz, not physical labour.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Imam Abu Hanifa's: fee is equal. This is confirmed by later authorities like Sakb al-Anhar.
Thus, in practice, partners share the fee equally.

Source Text

صاحبین کے قیاس کا جواب

Book of Division. Translation: And the proof of Abu Hanifa is that the fee is the compensation for separating, and distinguishing is not variable, and sometimes the calculation becomes heavy considering the small share, and sometimes the opposite occurs, so its consideration becomes impossible, then the ruling is attached to the original act of distinguishing, unlike digging a well because the fee is for moving soil, and moving soil varies (due to quantity). Commentary: In division, each person's share is separated, so just as the small is separated from the large, so also the large from the small, so the distinction of small and large is equal, and the fee is equal. Sometimes a small share complicates the calculation, and sometimes the opposite, so the basis remains on distinction which has no variation. As for digging a well, it is different because the fee is for removing soil, and there is variation between small and large, e.g., water is found at 20 yards, one's share is 3/4 and the other's 1/4, so one owes fee for digging 15 yards and the other 5 yards. Because 15 yards is heavier than 5 yards; in well-digging, there is variation based on quantity, so fee varies, unlike the first case where the purpose is distinction.
Response to Sahibayn's qiyas: And measurement and weighing, if for division, it is said it is on the same difference; if not for division, the fee is exchange for the work of measuring and weighing, and it varies. Translation: And measurement and weighing, if for division, it is said that the same difference applies; if not for division, the fee is in exchange for the act of measuring and weighing, and it varies. Commentary: Sahibayn analogized to the issue of measuring and weighing, so the response is that measuring and weighing are of two types: for division or not for division. In the first case, the same previous difference applies: according to Imam Sahib, the fee is according to the number of heads, and according to Sahibayn, according to share, so analogy is not valid because it is not accepted by Imam Sahib. And if measuring and weighing are not for division but to ascertain the quantity of what is measured/weighed, then the fee is according to share because the fee is compensation for the act of measuring/weighing, which varies with quantity. Example: Zaid and Bakr bought a heap of grain, one's share is 1/3 and the other's 2/3; they want to know its weight, division not intended, so fee will be in proportion: one pays 1/3, the other 2/3. In Sakb al-Anhar vol. 2 p. 419, Imam Sahib's statement is declared correct, and it is said: because the contractual object is distinction, not anything else, as in al-Mudmarat.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the fee for dividing jointly owned property. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the fee should be shared equally among the partners because the service performed—distinguishing (tamyiz) each share from the others—is uniform in effort regardless of the size of the share. He compares it to counting heads: the fee for a measurer or divider is for the act of differentiation, which does not vary. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) analogize to digging a well, where the fee varies with the amount of earth moved, or to weighing/measuring for sale, where effort increases with quantity. Abu Hanifa responds that the analogy fails because in division, the fee is for the act of distinguishing, not for moving soil or measuring quantity. Moreover, if measuring/weighing is done for the purpose of division, the same dispute applies; but if done merely to determine quantity (not for division), then the fee is proportional. The author of Sakb al-Anhar declares Imam Abu Hanifa's view correct, stating that the contractual object is the act of distinction, and nothing else. Thus, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the division fee is equal among partners regardless of their share size.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Division of jointly owned property to assign each partner's specific share.
اجرت (ujrah): Fee paid to a person who performs the division, e.g., a measurer or apportioner.
تميز (tamyiz): The act of distinguishing one share from another in the division process.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only on the partner who demands division
D) According to the effort involved in the division

Key Takeaway
The fee for division is equal among partners because the contractual object is the act of distinguishing (tamyiz), which does not vary with share size.

Ruling on Fee When Not Specified
Ruling on fee determination in absolute (mutlaq) contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for determining the fee in a contract of measuring/weighing when the fee is not specified.

Core Idea
An unspecified fee for measuring/weighing is apportioned proportionally to each partner's share of goods.

In Hanafi fiqh, when two or more partners hire a measurer or weigher and the fee is not explicitly agreed upon, the default rule is not equal division. Instead, the fee is based on the actual effort required, which varies with the quantity of goods. This lesson unpacks the rationale and application of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the contract
Determine that the contract is for measuring or weighing goods (kayl or wazan) and that the fee was left absolute (mutlaq) without specification.
2
Recognize the default rule
Understand that when unspecified, the fee is not equal but proportional to each partner's share of the goods, because the effort (mashaqqa) varies with quantity.
The matn calls this an 'excuse' (uzr) meaning a valid reason for differentiation.
3
Apply the proportional calculation
Calculate the fee by dividing it according to the share of goods each partner owns. For example, if A has 1/3 and B has 2/3, A pays 1/3 of the fee and B pays 2/3.
This contrasts with a fee for division (qismah), which is uniform regardless of share.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذر لو اطلق ولا يفصـ
ترجمہ اور یہی عذر ہے اگر مطلق بولا جائے اور اسمیل بیان نہ کی جائے
تشریح یعنی اگر کیال اور وزان پر اجرت لیتے وقت یہ بات طے نہ ہوئی تو اب کیا ہوگا۔ اجرت کس حساب سے واجب ہو گی ؟ تو فرماتے ہیں کہ یہاں یہی عذر ہے کہ قلیل و کثیر میں بظاہر تفاوت ہے یعنی زیادہ مقدار کی کیل میں مشقت زیادہ ہے تو زیادہ حصہ والے پر اجرت زیادہ ہوگی۔ تو اس کو فرمایا کہ اطلاق یعنی عدم تفصیل کے وقت بقدر ملک جو حساب رکھا گیا ہے وہ عذر کی بناء پر ہے۔ کہ تعین ہے نہیں اور بظاہر عمل میں تفاوت ہے جو اجرت میں تفاوت کو مقتضی ۔ ں ہے۔
And this is the excuse if it is said absolutely and not detailed. Translation: And this is the excuse if it is said absolutely and no explanation is given. Commentary: That is, if at the time of hiring the measurer and weigher the matter was not agreed upon, now what will happen? On what basis will the fee be obliged? So it is said that here this is the excuse: that between small and large there is apparent variation, meaning that measuring a larger quantity involves more hardship, so the one with the larger share pays more fee. So it is said that at the time of absolute (non-detail), the calculation according to share is due to this excuse, because there is no specification, and apparently the work varies, which necessitates variation in fee.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling for a scenario where the fee for measuring or weighing is not explicitly agreed upon. The matn states that if the contract is absolute (no details), then the same 'excuse' (uzr) applies: since the effort varies with quantity, the fee is divided proportionally to each partner's share of the goods. The commentary explains that when no specific arrangement was made, the default is to base the fee on the apparent variation in work—more quantity means more effort, so more fee. This is contrasted with the earlier dispute where the fee is for the act of division, which is uniform. Here, the fee is directly for the work of measuring/weighing, which does vary. Thus, in the absence of a stipulation, the fee follows proportionality.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): An unconditional or unspecified contract regarding the fee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is not payable until a fixed amount is agreed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an unspecified fee for measuring/weighing is divided proportionally to the share of goods due to variation in effort.

Wages for Measurement in Division of Property
Ruling on wage for measurement when unspecified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wages for measuring/weighing in division of property and distinguish between the general rule and the exception based on demand.

Core Idea
Wage for measuring is proportional to share, but if only some demand division, the demander bears the cost.

This passage from the chapter on division (al-qismah) addresses a practical problem: when partners divide property and need to hire someone to measure or weigh, who pays? The answer depends on whether the wage was specified and whether all partners agreed to the division. The commentator explains two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa and concludes the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the ruling applies when property is divided among co-owners and measuring/weighing is needed but no wage has been agreed upon beforehand.
The wage here is for the measurer's labor, not for the property itself.
2
State the general principle
According to the relied-upon view, the wage is proportional to each partner's share because a larger share requires more work (istihsan based on 'udhr).
The text says 'بقدر سہام' (according to shares).
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
A second narration from Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the wage is entirely on those who demanded division, not on those who refused, due to benefit and harm.
This is a weaker riwayah; the mufta bihi is the general principle.
4
Understand the condition
The exception only applies when some partners request division and others refuse. If all request, the wage is shared equally or proportionally.
The text says 'اگر بعض بٹوارہ کی درخواست کریں اور بعض انکار کریں' (if some request and some refuse).
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The prevalent view (asahh) is that wage is according to shares, as stated in the text 'بقول اصبح بالا جماع'.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

And this is the same excuse if spoken absolutely and not specified. Chapter of Division. Explanation: That is, if when taking wages for weighing and measuring, this matter was not settled, then what will happen? On what basis will the wage be obligatory? Then they say that here this is the excuse: in little and much there is apparently a difference, meaning in measuring a larger quantity there is more difficulty, so the one with a larger share will have a higher wage. So it was said that at the time of absolute (speech) i.e., without specification, the calculation made according to share is based on this excuse. That it is not specified, and apparently there is a difference in the action which necessitates a difference in wage. The second narration of Imam Sahib: ... translation: And there is a narration from Abu Hanifa that the wage is on the demander, not on the one who refuses, due to the benefit of the demander and the harm of the refuser. Explanation: This is the second narration from Imam Sahib: that the wage is obligatory only on the one who demanded, and not on the one who refused the division, because the desire of the demander was fulfilled so his benefit reached him, and the refuser suffered harm, so according to the benefit, the compensation should be on the demander only. But this can only be in the case where some request division and some refuse; otherwise if all request, then the answer is that the wage will be equal on all or according to share. So the summary is that in the case of measuring and weighing, according to the prevalent view, the wage will be according to shares. As in Sirkab al-Anhar.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on Division. It addresses the problem of determining wages for measuring and weighing when no specific arrangement has been made. The commentator explains that the wage is proportionate to the share because greater quantity implies greater work. Then a second narration from Imam Abu Hanifa is mentioned: if some partners request division and others refuse, the wage is entirely on the demander due to benefit. However, if all request, the wage is shared equally or proportionally. The passage concludes that the relied-upon position (al-asahh) is that wage is according to shares in measurement cases.

Key Terms
اجرت (ujrat): Wage or fee for the work of measuring/weighing during division.
عذر (udhr): An excuse or reason for a ruling, here justifying wage proportionality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The demander only
C) The refuser only
D) Proportionally according to shares

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on unspecified measuring wage: proportional to shares by default, but if only some demand division, the demander bears the cost per the second narration.

Response to Sahibayn's Objections in Property Division
Intra-Hanafi dispute over benefit of evidence and analogy in division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on the benefit of evidence in division of land, explain the distinction between land and movables, and evaluate Sahibayn's qiyas.

Core Idea
Evidence remains beneficial even after acknowledgment, and land's inherent preservation breaks the analogy to movables.

Sahibayn raised two objections to Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that division of inherited land can proceed without establishing ownership evidence. Their first objection claimed evidence is futile when all parties acknowledge the claim. Their second analogized land division to movable property or a sale. This lesson unpacks Abu Hanifa's responses: evidence is still beneficial because one heir is appointed as a substitute opponent, and land is inherently preserved and not liable, unlike movables. Understanding these distinctions is key to mastering Hanafi qismah law.

Guided Path
1
State Sahibayn's first objection
Identify the claim that establishing evidence (bayyinah) is futile when all co-heirs acknowledge the property claim. This objection is based on the idea that evidence is only needed when there is a dispute.
Sahibayn argued that since all parties agree, evidence serves no purpose.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's response to the first objection
Note that one heir is appointed as a substitute (khasm) for the deceased, and evidence is presented against that opponent. Acknowledgment does not prevent this, just as a creditor can present witnesses despite an heir's acknowledgment of a debt.
The key analogy: the creditor's evidence is accepted even after acknowledgment.
3
State Sahibayn's second objection (qiyas)
Identify the analogy: dividing land is like dividing movable property (which requires evidence) or like a sale (where division is a judgment against the seller).
Sahibayn used qiyas to argue that evidence is required in all these cases.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's distinction for the second objection
Recognize that land is self-preserving (muhsan bi nafsihi) and not liable (ghayr madmun) in the hands of a possessor, unlike movables which require preservation and carry liability. Also, in a sale, ownership transfers immediately, so division is not a judgment against a third party.
This is qiyas ma'a al-fariq: an analogy with a difference, thus invalid.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
Conclude that Abu Hanifa's view is relied upon; Sahibayn's objections are rejected. The judge can divide land among co-owners without requiring them to prove each other's ownership.
The tarjih authority is al-Ashraf, who deems Sahibayn's qiyas as flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحبین کی دلیل کا جواب
وهو مفيد لان بعض الورثة ينتصب خصما عن المورث ولا يمتنع ذالك بإقراره كما في الوارث والوصي المقر بالدين فانه يقبل البيئة عليه مع اقراره
ترجمہ اور یہ (اقامت بینہ ) مفید ہے اسلئے کہ بعض ورثا کو مورث کی جانب سے خصم مقرر کیا جائے گا اور یہ منع نہیں ہے اس کے اقرار کی وجہ سے جیسا کہ وارث یا اس وصی کے اندر جو دین کا اقرار کرنے والا ہو کہ اس کا بینہ اس پر قبول کر لیا جاتا ہے اس کے اقرار کے باوجود ۔ تشریح صاحبین نے فرمایا تھا کہ یہاں اقامت بینہ مفید نہیں بلکہ لغو ہے۔ اس کا جواب دیا جا رہا ہے کہ اقامت بینہ یہاں مفید ہے اسلئے کہ شرکاء میں سے ایک کو یہاں میت کا نائب بنا دیا جائے گا اور دوسرا اس کا خصم بنے گا تا کہ اب قاضی فیصلہ کر سکے۔
سوال ... جب ان دونوں کو اقرار ہے پھر ایک کو خصم بنانے کا کیا مطلب اور اس کا کیا فائدہ؟ جواب اقرار کے باوجود بھی آدمی خصم بن سکتا ہے جیسے کسی میت کا کوئی وارث یا ڈسی ہے اور کسی نے میت کے اوپر اپنے قرض کا دعویٰ کیا اور وارث یا وصی نے اس کا اقرار کر لیا مگر صاحب دین چاہتا ہے کہ قاضی کے سامنے پھر بھی اپنے دعوئی پر گواہ پیش کروں تا کہ بات اور پختہ ہو جائے تو اس کے بینہ کو قبول کر لیا جائے گا۔
تو دیکھئے یہاں اقرار وارث یا وصی موجود ہے پھر اقامت بین متنع نہیں۔ اسی طرح اگر چہ دونوں وارثوں کا ایک ہی دعوئی اور ایک ہی اقرار ہے اس اقرار کے باوجود خصم بنا نا ممتنع نہیں ہے۔ جب خصم بنانا منع نہ ہوا تو اقامت بینہ کا مفید ہونا ثابت ہو گیا اسلئے کہ بینہ خصم کے مقابلہ میں پیش کیا جاتا ہے اور وہ صورت یہاں موجود ہے۔ اس مقام پر ملا چلی کا اعتراض ہے اور اس کا نہیں جواب ہے۔ تفصیل کیلئے دیکھئے نتائج الافکار فتنہ فتح القدیری ۷ ج ۸ ۔
صاحبین کے قیاس کا جواب
بخلاف المنقول لان فى القسمة نظراً لحاجة الى الحفظ اما العقار محصن بنفسه ولان المنقول مضمون . على من وقع في يده ولا كذالك العقار عنده وبخلاف المشترى لان السيع لا يبقى على ذالك البائع وان لم يقسم فلم تكن القسمة قضاء على الغير
ترجمہ بخلاف منقول کے اسلئے کہ قسمت میں شفقت ہے حفاظت کی جانب حاجت کی وجہ سے بہر حال زمین خود محفوظ ہے اور اسلئے کہ منقول مضمون ہے۔ اس شخص پر جس کے قبضہ میں وہ واقع ہوئی اور زمین ایسی نہیں ہے ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اور بخلاف مشتری کے اسلئے کہ مبیع بائع کی ملکیت پر برقرار نہیں رہتی اگر چہ تقسیم نہ کی جائے تو قسمت قضاء علی الخیر نہ ہوگا۔ تشریح صاحبین نے پہلی صورت کو دوسری اور تیسری صورت پر قیاس کیا ہے اس کا بنواب دے رہے ہیں کہ یہ قیاس صحیح نہیں ہے اس
لیے کہ شی منقول میں حفاظت کی حاجت ہوتی ہے۔ بخلاف زمین کے کیونکہ وہ تو خود محفوظ ہوتی ہے۔ دوسری وجہ یہ بھی ہے کہ شی منقول جس کے قبضہ میں جائے گی اس کا وہ مضامین ہوگا تو آسمیں میت کیلئے شفقت ہے اور زمین جس کے قبضہ میں ہو وہ اس کا ضامن نہ ہو گا۔ اس لئے ابو حنیفہ نے فرمایا کہ زمین کا غصب تحقیق نہیں ہوتا۔ بہر حال اس گفتگو سے معلوم ہوا کہ زمین کا کے مسئلہ کو منقول کے اوپر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں ہے۔ اور رہا شراء والا مسئلہ، تو چونکہ جب بائع نے کوئی چیز بیچ دن تو اب وہ اس کا مالک نہیں رہا۔ اگر چہ خرید نے والوں نے آپس میں ابھی ہوارہ بھی نہ کیا ہو تو یہاں قضاء علی الغیر لازم نہیں آئے گی اور پہلی صورت میں قضاء علی المیت لازم آتی ہے اس وجہ سے آخر کے دونوں مسئلوں کو اول پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے۔
Response to Sahibayn's argument: 'And it (establishing evidence) is beneficial because some heirs are appointed as opponents on behalf of the deceased, and that is not prevented by his acknowledgment, just as in the case of an heir or executor who acknowledges a debt: evidence is accepted against him despite his acknowledgment.' Commentary: Sahibayn said establishing evidence here is useless. The response is that it is beneficial because one of the co-owners is made a substitute for the deceased and the other becomes his opponent so that the judge can decide. Question: When both acknowledge, what is the meaning of making one an opponent? Answer: A person can be an opponent despite acknowledgment, as when an heir or executor acknowledges a debt and the creditor wants to present witnesses to strengthen the case, his evidence is accepted. Thus, despite acknowledgment, making an opponent is not prevented. When that is established, the benefit of establishing evidence is proven because evidence is presented against an opponent, and that scenario exists here. There is an objection from Mulla Chali but no answer; see Nata'ij al-Afkar and Fath al-Qadir vol. 7 p. 8. Response to Sahibayn's analogy: 'Unlike movable property, because in division there is consideration of the need for preservation; immovable property is inherently preserved, and because movable property is guaranteed (liable) for the one in whose hand it falls, while immovable property is not like that according to Abu Hanifa, and unlike a buyer because the sold item does not remain the property of the seller even if not divided, so division is not a judgment against others.' Commentary: Sahibayn analogized the first case to the second and third cases; the response is that this analogy is incorrect because movable property requires preservation, unlike land which is inherently preserved. Second, movable property is liable, but land is not liable. Therefore Abu Hanifa said that usurpation of land is not established. Thus, analogizing land to movables is incorrect. As for the case of purchase, when the seller sells, he is no longer the owner even if the buyers have not yet divided among themselves, so there is no judgment against others; but in the first case, there is a judgment against the deceased, so analogizing the last two cases to the first is an analogy with a difference.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. It addresses two objections raised by Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) against Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling regarding division of property among co-heirs. First objection: Sahibayn argued that establishing evidence (bayyinah) is futile because both parties acknowledge the claim. The Hanafi response clarifies that one heir is appointed as a substitute for the deceased (as a defendant), and the other becomes plaintiff; evidence is presented against the opponent, and acknowledgment does not prevent this, analogous to a creditor seeking witnesses despite an heir's acknowledgment. Second objection: Sahibayn analogized the case of dividing land to dividing movable property or to a sale. The Hanafi response distinguishes: land is self-preserving and not liable (daman) in the hands of a possessor, unlike movables which require preservation and carry liability. Also, in a sale, the seller ceases to be owner immediately, so division is not a judgment against a third party; but in division of inheritance, the judgment is against the deceased's estate. Hence the analogy fails. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, particularly witness testimony presented in court to establish a claim.
قسمة (qismah): Division of jointly owned property among co-sharers, either by agreement or court order.
خصم (khasm): Adversary or opponent in a legal proceeding; here, one heir is appointed as a substitute defendant for the deceased.
قياس مع الفارق (qiyas ma'a al-fariq): Analogy with a difference; an invalid analogy because the two cases differ in a legally relevant aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Abu Hanifa hold that land is not analogous to movable property in the context of division?
A) Because the value of land is higher.
B) Because land is self-preserving and not liable, while movables require preservation and are liable.
C) Because movable property is always divided by agreement.
D) Because division of land requires a judge, while movables do not.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's position that evidence remains beneficial despite acknowledgment and that land's inherent preservation invalidates the analogy to movables is the relied-upon view in Hanafi qismah.

Diverging Narrations on Division Without Specifying Ownership Source
Intra-Hanafi comparison of two narrations regarding division when parties claim ownership without stating how

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi narrations regarding division of property among claimants and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
If claimants do not acknowledge a third party's right, the judge can divide their land — the stronger narration allows it without proof of ownership.

This passage compares two conflicting narrations within the Hanafi school on whether a judge may divide property between two claimants who claim ownership but do not explain how they acquired it. The key principle is whether division constitutes a judgment against an absent third party (qada 'ala al-ghayr). Let us examine both positions and identify the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations
Locate the source of each narration: first from Kitab al-Qismah (al-Mabsut, cited by al-Quduri and al-Hidayah); second from al-Jami' al-Saghir.
The first is from the Zahir al-Riwayah, the core collection.
2
State the ruling of each
The first says the judge divides the land; the second says the judge does not divide until proof of ownership is shown.
3
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The first relies on the absence of acknowledgment of a third party (no qada 'ala al-ghayr). The second relies on the possibility (ihtimal) that the land belongs to someone else.
Both are valid istidlal within the school.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the first, division is quick; under the second, parties must present evidence of ownership, causing delay.
Practical consequence in court.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The first narration is relied upon because it is from the principal books (Zahir al-Riwayah) and its reasoning is stronger. This is the operative position for fatwa.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah reinforces this preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وان ادعوا الملك ولم يذكروا كيف انتقل اليهم قسمه بينهم لانه ليس في القسمة قضاء على الغير لانهم ما اقرو ابا لملك لغيرهم قال هذه رواية كتاب القسمة
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور اگر انہوں نے ملک کا دعوی کیا اور انہوں نے یہ ذکر نہیں کیا کہ اس کی طرف یہ کیسے منتقل ہوئی تو قاضی اس کو ان کے درمیان تقسیم کر دے گا اسلئے کہ اس قسمت میں قضاء علی الغیر نہیں ہے اسلئے انہوں نے اپنے غیر کیلئے ملک کا اقرار نہیں کیا صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ یہ مبسوط کی کتاب القسمت کی روایت ہے۔
وفى الجامع الصغير ارض دعاها رجلان واقاما البينة انها فى ايديهما وارادا القسمة لم يقسمها البينة انها لهما لاحتمال ان تكون لغيرهما
ترجمہ اور جامع صغیر میں ہے کہ ایک زمین ہے جس کے بارے میں دو شخصوں نے دعوی کیا اور دونوں نے بینہ قائم کیا اور یہ ان کے قبضہ میں ہے اور دونوں نے قسمت کا ارادہ کیا تو قاضی اس کو تقسیم نہیں کرے گا یہاں تک کہ دونوں بینہ قائم کریں۔ اس بات پر کہ یہ زمین ان دونو سات سے اس بات کے استمال کی وجہ سے کہ وہ ان کے غیر کی ہو۔
He said: 'And if they claim ownership and did not mention how it transferred to them, then the judge shall divide it among them, because in this division there is no judgment against others, since they did not acknowledge ownership for someone else.' He said: 'This is the narration of Kitab al-Qismah.' Translation: Al-Quduri said: If they claim ownership and do not state how it was transferred to them, the judge divides it among them, because there is no judgment against others, as they did not acknowledge ownership for anyone else. The author of al-Hidayah said: This is the narration of Kitab al-Qismah from al-Mabsut. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'Land which two men claim and they establish evidence that it is in their hands, and they desire division, the judge shall not divide it until they establish evidence that it belongs to them, due to the possibility that it belongs to others.'
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between two narrations. The first narration, from Kitab al-Qismah of al-Mabsut (and cited by al-Quduri and al-Hidayah), rules that if co-owners claim ownership of land without explaining how they acquired it, the judge can divide it among them. The reasoning is that they have not acknowledged ownership for any third party, so the division does not constitute a judgment against an absent party. The second narration, from al-Jami' al-Saghir, rules the opposite: when two claimants each present evidence that the land is in their possession (e.g., they hold it) and seek division, the judge should not divide until they also present evidence that the land belongs to them (i.e., ownership), because there is a possibility it might belong to someone else. The reliance (mufta bihi) is on the first narration, as it is from the more authoritative source (Zahir al-Riwayah of al-Mabsut), though both narrations are recognized. The key difference is whether the mere claim of possession (without proof of ownership) suffices for division; the first allows it, the second requires proof of ownership to avoid potential loss to a third party.

Key Terms
قضاء على الغير (qada' 'ala al-ghayr): A judicial ruling that affects an absent third party's rights; such rulings are generally prohibited in Hanafi law unless the absent party is represented.
احتمال (ihtimal): Possibility; here, the possibility that the land belongs to someone other than the claimants, which prevents division until ownership is proven.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the narration from al-Jami' al-Saghir require evidence of ownership before dividing land among two claimants?
A) Because the claimants must clarify how they acquired the land.
B) Because there is a possibility the land belongs to someone else.
C) Because the judge needs both parties to agree on the division.
D) Because the land might be immovable property.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the mufta bihi is that division may proceed without proof of ownership if no third party claim is acknowledged, based on the stronger Zahir al-Riwayah narration.

Distinction Between Heirs and Co-Purchasers in Division: The Role of Ownership Type
Ruling with proof: distinction between inheritance and purchase in division cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on division of co-purchased property when one co-purchaser is absent and explain the legal distinction between milk al-khilafa and milk mubtada' that underlies it.

Core Idea
A present co-purchaser cannot represent an absent one because their ownership is independent (milk mubtada'), unlike heirs who share successor ownership (milk al-khilafa).

This lesson explores a key procedural distinction in Hanafi law: why a judge can divide inherited property among heirs even if one heir is absent, but cannot do the same for co-purchasers. The answer lies in the nature of ownership—whether it is 'successor ownership' (milk al-khilafa) tied to the deceased, or 'original ownership' (milk mubtada') acquired independently. This distinction affects not only representation in court but also the right to return a defective item and other legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage contrasts inheritance (heirs) and purchase (co-purchasers). For each, note the ruling regarding division in the absence of one owner.
Heirs can divide even if one is absent; co-purchasers cannot.
2
State the ruling for co-purchasers
The judge does not (la yuqsim) divide co-purchased property when one co-purchaser is absent. This is the core ruling.
3
Understand the key concepts
Define milk al-khilafa (successor ownership) as ownership transferred from a predecessor with same rights and liabilities, and milk mubtada' (original ownership) as independent ownership without chain.
Heirs have milk al-khilafa; purchasers have milk mubtada'.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Trace why representation is possible in inheritance but not in purchase. In inheritance, one heir stands for the deceased (and thus the absent heir) because they share the chain. In purchase, no such chain exists, so one cannot represent another.
The present heir becomes a litigant on behalf of the deceased; the present purchaser only for himself.
5
Apply the distinction to similar cases
Consider other situations like khiyar al-ayb (option of defect). An heir can return defective property the deceased bought; a co-purchaser cannot return for his co-purchaser's share.
This reinforces the independence of milk mubtada'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانوا مشتريين لم يقسم مع غيبة احدهم والفرق ان ملک الوارث ملک خلافة حتى يرد بالعيب، ويرد عليه بالعيب فيما اشتراه المورث اوباع ويصير مغرورا بشراء المورث فانتصب احدهما خصما عن الميت فيما في يده والآخر عن نفسه فصارت القسمة قضاء بحضرة المتخاصمين اما الملك الثابت بالشرا ملك مبتداء ولهذا لايرد بالعيب على بائع بائعه فلا يصلح الحاضر خصما عن الغائب فوضح الفرق
And if they are purchasers, the judge does not divide in the absence of one of them. The difference is that the owner of inheritance is a successor ownership (milk al-khilafa), such that he can return (the property) due to a defect, and it can be returned to him due to a defect in what the testator purchased or sold, and he becomes defrauded by the purchase of the testator. So one of them becomes a litigant on behalf of the deceased regarding what is in his hand, and the other on behalf of himself, so the division becomes a judgment in the presence of litigants. As for ownership established by purchase, it is an original ownership (milk mubtada'), and for this reason, he cannot return it due to a defect to the seller of his seller. So the present (heir) is not fit to be a litigant on behalf of the absent (co-purchaser). Thus the difference is clear.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage explains the legal reasoning behind the rule that when co-purchasers (people who jointly bought a property) seek division and one of them is absent, the judge does not act. The key distinction lies in the nature of ownership. A heir's ownership is described as 'milk al-khilafa'—successor ownership, which is derived from the deceased. The heir stands in the shoes of the deceased, meaning the heir can claim defects (khiyar al-ayb) against the seller, just as the deceased could. Also, the heir becomes 'makhrook' (defrauded) by the deceased's purchase, meaning the deceased's purchase affects the heir's rights. Therefore, in a dispute among heirs, one present heir can represent the deceased (as a litigant) because they share the same chain of ownership. In contrast, a co-purchaser's ownership is 'milk mubtada''—original ownership, independent of any predecessor. Each purchaser owns a separate share. One co-purchaser cannot represent an absent co-purchaser because there is no legal connection between them beyond the shared property. The present co-purchaser cannot act as a litigant on behalf of the absent one, and thus the division cannot proceed without the absent party. The commentator summarizes: 'Thus the difference is clear.'

Key Terms
ملك خلافة (milk al-khilafa): Successor ownership: ownership that is transferred from a predecessor (the deceased) to the heir, maintaining the same legal attributes and liabilities.
ملك مبتدأ (milk mubtada'): Original ownership: ownership that is acquired independently, without a chain from a previous owner, such as by direct purchase.
خصم (khasm): A litigant or adversary in a legal proceeding; one who argues on behalf of a party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can an heir represent the deceased in court but a co-purchaser cannot represent an absent co-purchaser?
A) Because heirs are more honest than co-purchasers
B) Because the heir's ownership is successor ownership (milk al-khilafa), while the co-purchaser's is original (milk mubtada')
C) Because the judge dislikes purchasers
D) Because co-purchasers always have disputes

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that heir's ownership (milk al-khilafa) allows representation of the deceased, while co-purchaser's ownership (milk mubtada') does not permit one co-owner to represent another.

Partition When a Buyer is Absent: Milk al-Khilafah vs. Milk Mubtada'
Explains why partition is prohibited when a buyer is absent, contrasting with inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on partition when one buyer is absent, explain the distinction between milk al-khilafah and milk mubtada', and apply this distinction to determine when judicial partition is valid.

Core Idea
A present buyer cannot represent an absent co-buyer for partition, because purchase creates new ownership, not representative succession.

In this passage, Imam al-Marghinani clarifies a subtle but crucial rule: when some co-owners are buyers (not heirs), the qadi will not partition the property if one buyer is absent—even if the present buyers bring witnesses. The reason lies in the nature of ownership. By contrasting purchase with inheritance, the author reveals how the Hanafi school safeguards the rights of absent parties. Understanding this distinction will sharpen your ability to apply principles of representation (niyabah) in fiqh al-qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being discussed? Judicial partition (qismah) when co-owners are buyers and one is absent. State the ruling: not permissible (ghayr ja'iz).
2
Locate the author's proof (dalil)
The proof is the distinction between two types of ownership: milk al-khilafah (inheritance) and milk mubtada' (purchase). The author illustrates with examples: return of defective goods, umm walad case.
These examples show that an heir steps into the deceased's legal position, but a buyer does not step into the seller's position.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof proves the ruling)
In inheritance, a present heir can represent an absent heir because both derive their ownership from the deceased (khilafah). In purchase, each buyer has independent ownership; no representation is possible. Thus partition would be qada' 'ala al-gha'ib, which is invalid.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement)
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) might allow partition if evidence is established, but the Hanafi school strictly prohibits it because the present buyer lacks authority to represent the absent one.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preferred position)
The Hanafi position is based on the fundamental rule that judgment against an absent person without representation is not permissible. This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling).
