And if they are buyers, he shall not divide in the absence of one of them. The difference is that the ownership of the heir is ownership of succession (milk al-khilafah), such that he can return due to defect and it is returned to him due to defect in what the deceased purchased or sold, and he becomes deceived by the purchase of the deceased. So one of them is appointed as a representative against the deceased for what is in his hand, and the other for himself, so the partition becomes a judgment in the presence of the litigants. But the ownership established by purchase is new ownership, therefore he cannot return due to defect against the seller of his seller, so the present one is not fit to be a representative against the absent one. Thus the difference is clear.

Translation: And if they are buyers, the judge will not divide in the absence of one of them. The difference is that the ownership of the heir is ownership of succession, such that he will return due to defect and it will be returned to him due to defect in what the deceased purchased or sold. And he will be deceived by the deceased's purchase, so one of them will be appointed as a representative against the deceased for what is in his hand, and the other for himself, so this partition becomes a judgment in the presence of two litigants. But what is established by purchase is new ownership, and for this reason he cannot return it due to defect against the seller of his seller, so the present one will not have the capacity to be a representative against the absent one. Thus the difference became clear.

Explanation: In the previous section, three issues were mentioned whose detailed explanation has passed. Then as the fourth issue, an absolute issue came that was not restricted to inheritance and purchase; its explanation has passed. Then as the fifth issue, inheritance was mentioned in which one heir was absent and partition was stated to be done. This is the sixth issue, whose detail is that two persons came to the qadi, and they have a house etc. in their possession. They claimed: We purchased this house from so-and-so, and we are three partners; one of us is absent, and we want partition. So will the qadi partition? He said: If all were present, the qadi would partition, but here he will not partition, even if the present partners establish witnesses to the purchase, still the qadi will not partition.

Question: In the first case when an heir was absent, you said to perform partition; why here refusal?

Answer: In the first case, one of the partners is appointed as a representative on behalf of the deceased, and here there is no possibility of representation. The seller has completely left the middle, and the present partner does not have the capacity to become the deputy and successor of the absent partner. So here judgment against an absent person (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib) becomes necessary, whereas in the first case there was no judgment against an absent person but rather judgment in the presence of litigants.

Question: How is the heir the successor of the deceased?

Answer: This is obvious: that the ownership of the heir is on the basis of succession. Its clear proof is that if the deceased purchased something and died, and the heir finds a defect in it, he can return it to the seller due to the defect. Similarly, if the deceased sold something and died, and the buyer finds a defect in it, he can return it to the heir due to the defect. Also, if the deceased purchased a slave-girl and died, and the heir made her umm walad, and then someone claimed entitlement, the child would be free, the value of the child is due from the heir for the claimant, and the 'aqr of the slave-girl is due. Then the heir will take back the value of the child and the price of the slave-girl from the seller, but he will not take back the 'aqr. And if the deceased himself were alive, he would have done the same as the heir did in his absence. So from this it is known that the heir's ownership is ownership of succession. In the inheritance issue, the reality was that one of them is appointed as a representative against the deceased in the matter of what is in his hand, and the other partner becomes a representative against the other for himself. For this reason, this case did not become judgment against an absent person. And the purchase case became judgment against an absent person, therefore the first is permissible and the second is not permissible. Because in purchase there is not ownership of succession but rather new ownership. That is the reason that the buyer can return to his seller due to defect, but he cannot make it due from the seller of his seller. For this reason we have differentiated between the two.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Partition in Hanafi fiqh. It addresses the scenario where some co-owners are buyers (purchasers) and one of them is absent. The author (al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah) rules that the qadi will not order partition in the absence of one of the buyers, even if the present buyers bring witnesses to prove their purchase. The reasoning is based on a fundamental distinction between two types of ownership: milk al-khilafah (ownership of succession) which applies to inheritance, and milk mubtada' (new ownership) which applies to purchase. In inheritance, the heir stands in the shoes of the deceased (khalifah), so a present heir can represent both himself and the absent heir as a litigant, making the partition a judgment in the presence of parties. In purchase, each buyer has an independent new ownership; the present buyer has no authority to represent the absent buyer, so partition would constitute judgment against an absent person (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib), which is invalid. The commentary then discusses a previous case where partition was allowed despite an absent heir, and answers the objection by emphasizing that representation is possible only through the chain of succession in inheritance, not in purchase. The illustrative examples (return of defective goods, umm walad case) reinforce the concept of milk al-khilafah. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that partition is not permissible in such purchase cases.

Key Terms
ملک الخلافة (milk al-khilafah): Ownership that passes from the deceased to the heir by succession, where the heir stands in the place of the deceased in legal transactions.
ملک مبتدأ (milk mubtada'): New ownership that begins with the buyer, independent of any prior owner.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): A judgment passed against an absent person who has no representative present to defend them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school prohibit partition when one of the co-owners (buyers) is absent?
A) Because the absent buyer's consent is not obtained.
B) Because the present buyer cannot represent the absent one in court, making it qada' 'ala al-gha'ib.
C) Because the property is not physically divided.
D) Because the purchase was not witnessed.

Key Takeaway
Judicial partition requires representation of all parties; in purchase, a present buyer cannot represent an absent co-buyer because purchase creates independent ownership (milk mubtada'), not succession (milk al-khilafah).

General Ruling on Partitionable Property
Introduction to what can and cannot be partitioned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a judge can order partition at the request of one partner and explain why partition is considered a binding right (haqq lazim).

Core Idea
Partition is an established right when each partner can benefit from his share after division.

In joint property, disputes over division are common. The Hanafi school sets a clear rule: if the property can be divided without destroying the benefit for any partner, then division is not just a courtesy but a binding legal right that can be enforced by a judge even if one partner refuses. This short passage from Al-Quduri establishes that foundational rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? It is the partition (qismat) of joint property. The ruling is that it is an established right (haqq lazim) when the condition is met.
2
Identify the condition (shart)
Find the key phrase: 'each partner can benefit from his share' (yantafi'u bi-nasibihi). This is the condition that makes partition enforceable.
Benefit here means practical use without loss of value.
3
Understand the legal consequence
When the condition is satisfied, partition becomes a haqq lazim — an established right that cannot be refused. The judge can order it at the request of even a single partner.
This is stated explicitly: 'because partition is an established right (haqq lazim) in what allows it at the request of one of them.'
4
Recognize the boundary cases
Not all property is partible. The passage implies that the property must 'admit partition' (yuh'tamalu al-qismah). If division would destroy benefit, the condition is not met, and partition is not compulsory.
The section will elaborate on which items are partible and which are not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يقسم وما لا يقسم
ترجمہ فصل ہے ان چیزوں کے بیان میں جن کو تقسیم کیا جائے گا اور جن کو تقسیم نہیں کیا جائے گا
تشریح قسمت کے مسائل ان دونوں پر قسم ہیں کہ ان میں سے بعض اشیاء قابل قسمت ہوتی ہیں اور بعض نہیں ہوتیں ، تو ان کا بیان ضریبی ہے تو اس فصل میں اس کی تفصیل بیان کی جائے گی ۔
شرکاء میں سے ہر ایک اپنے حصے سے انتفاع کر سکتا ہے تو قاضی ان میں سے ایک کے مطالبہ پر بھی تقسیم کر سکتا ہے
قال واذا كان كل واحد من الشركاء ينتفع بنصيبه قسم بطلب احدهم لان القسمة حق لازم فيما يحتملها عند طلب احدهم على ما بيناه من قبل
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جبکہ شرکاء میں سے ہر ایک اپنے حصہ سے انتفاع حاصل کر سکتا ہے تو قاضی ان میں سے ایک کی طلب پر تقسیم کر دے گا (اگر چہ دوسرا انکار بھی کرے) اس لئے کہ ان چیزوں میں تقسیم کا احتمال رکھتی ہیں حق لازم ہے ان میں سے کسی ایک کی
Section on what is partitioned and what is not. Translation: This is a section explaining the things that will be partitioned and those that will not. Explanation: The issues of partition are of two types: some items are partible and some are not. So their description is necessary, and in this section, its detail will be explained. If each partner can benefit from his share, the judge can partition at the request of one of them. He said: And if each of the partners can benefit from his share, he will partition at the request of one of them, because partition is an established right in what allows it at the request of one of them, as we have explained before. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And if each of the partners can benefit from his share, the judge will partition at the request of one of them (even if the other refuses), because in those things that admit partition, it is an established right at the request of one of them.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (fasl) on what can and cannot be partitioned. The text explains that partition issues are divided into two categories: items that are capable of division and those that are not. The general ruling from al-Quduri is that if each partner can benefit from his share after division, then partition is an established right (haqq lazim) that can be enforced at the request of a single partner, even if the other refuses. This is the default rule: partition is obligatory when the property is divisible and each share remains beneficial. The details of which items are partible and which are not will be elaborated in this section.

Key Terms
قسمت (qismat): Partition of joint property into distinct shares.
انتفاع (intifa'): Beneficial use; the ability to derive benefit from one's share.
حق لازم (haqq lazim): An established right that is binding and enforceable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) When the property is movable.
D) When the property is located in the court's jurisdiction.

Key Takeaway
Partition of joint property is an established right (haqq lazim) enforceable at the request of a single partner if each partner can benefit from his share after division.

When Partition Can Be Compelled: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Ruling on partition when one owner's share is too small to benefit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on partition when one partner's share is too small to benefit and differentiate the views of al-Jassas and al-Hakim al-Shahid, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A partner with a too-small share cannot compel partition because the law does not assist an obstinate request.

Partition of jointly owned property is generally a necessary right that can be enforced at the request of any co-owner—provided each can benefit. But what if one owner's share is so small that division would harm him? This passage from al-Hidayah presents the Hanafi master rule and then layers in differing opinions from within the school. Understanding this dispute clarifies how a qadi balances benefit, harm, and the principle of not wasting resources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule of partition
State the general Hanafi principle: if each partner benefits from his share after division, partition is a necessary right (haqq lazim) and can be compelled at the request of any one partner.
This applies only to property that can be divided without significant loss (muhtamal al-qisma).
2
Recognize the exception for unequal benefit
When one partner benefits and the other is harmed due to a very small share, only the benefiting partner (large-share owner) can demand partition; the small-share owner's request is considered obstinate (muta'annit) and is not honored.
The small-share owner's request leads to waste and hardship for himself and others.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare the master view with the positions of al-Jassas (opposite – large owner intends harm, small owner accepts harm) and al-Hakim al-Shahid (either request granted). Note the reasoning behind each.
Al-Jassas focuses on the large owner's intent to harm; the master view focuses on objective benefit.
4
Determine the tarjih and mufta bihi
The author of al-Hidayah (following Quduri and the majority of the early Hanafi masters) declares the first view the most correct (al-asahh) and the relied-upon position for fatwa.
The small-share owner may not compel partition; the large-share owner may.
5
Apply the rule to a concrete scenario
Given two co-owners A (90% share) and B (10%), both requesting partition, determine the qadi's decision under Hanafi law: A's request is granted, B's is denied.
If B wants to sell his share, he must do so as an undivided share.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يقسم وما لا يقسم
قال واذا كان كل واحد من الشركاء ينتفع بنصيبه قسم بطلب احدهم لان القسمة حق لازم فيما يحتملها عند طلب احدهم على ما بيناه من قبل
وإن كان ينتفع احدهما ويستضر به الآخر لقل نصيبه فان طلب صاحب الكثير قسم وان طلب صاحب القليل لم يقسم لان الاول منتفع به فاعتبر طلبه والثاني متعنت في طلبه فلم يعتبر وذكر الجصاص على قلب هذا لان صاحب الكثير يريد الاضرار بغيره والآخر يرضى بضرر نفسه وذكر الحاكم الشهيد في مختصره ان ايهما طلب القسمة يقسم القاضى والوجه اندرج فيما ذكرناه والاصح المذكور في الكتاب وهو الاول
Chapter on what is divided and what is not divided. He said: And if each of the partners benefits from his share, it is divided at the request of one of them, because division is a necessary right in what can bear it at the request of one of them, as we explained before. And if one of them benefits and the other is harmed due to the smallness of his share, then if the one with the large share requests, it is divided; and if the one with the small share requests, it is not divided, because the first is benefited by it, so his request is considered, and the second is obstinate in his request, so it is not considered. And al-Jassas mentioned the opposite of this, because the one with the large share intends to harm the other, and the other is satisfied with harming himself. And al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned in his Mukhtasar that whichever of them requests division, the qadi divides; and the correct [view] is what is mentioned in the book, which is the first.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage from the chapter 'What is Divided and What is Not Divided' discusses the conditions under which a qadi may compel partition. The general rule: if each partner can benefit from his share after division, partition is a necessary right (haqq lazim) and can be enforced at the request of even one partner, regardless of the other's objection. However, if one partner's share is so small that he would derive no benefit (e.g., a tiny plot of land), while the other's share is large and beneficial, then a dispute arises. The relied-upon (asahh) position in the matn is that only the partner with the large share can demand partition; the small-share partner's request is considered obstinate (muta'annit) and is not granted. Imam al-Jassas (a Hanafi scholar) reversed this, arguing that the large-share partner seeks to harm the other, while the small-share partner consents to harm himself, so his request should be honored. Al-Hakim al-Shahid (a later Hanafi) opined that either partner's request should be granted. The author of al-Hidayah (and the matn) declares the first view (that of Quduri) as the most correct (al-asahh). The practical result: if the small-share partner wants division, the qadi will not compel it; the large-share partner may compel division because he benefits.

Key Terms
متعنت (muta'annit): One who makes a request that leads to his own harm and waste, considered obstructive.
متحمل القسمة (muhtamal al-qisma): Property that can be divided without causing significant loss or harm to the owners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The qadi does not grant partition because the requester cannot benefit.
C) The qadi only grants if the other co-owner agrees.
D) The qadi appoints an appraiser to decide.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule on partition balances benefit and harm: only a co-owner who would benefit from division can compel it; a request that leads to waste is not honored.

The Partition Dispute: Whose Request Counts When One Share Is Too Small?
Three opinions on partition when one partner suffers due to small share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on partition when one partner's share is too small and identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
When partition harms one partner, only the benefiting partner's request is accepted.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when jointly owned property cannot be divided without rendering one partner's share useless, who can compel partition? The passage presents three views and settles on the position that the partner who benefits may request division, while the one who would suffer cannot. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi emphasis on benefit and intention in property law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Two partners co-own property; one has a large share that remains beneficial after partition, the other has a small share that becomes useless after partition. One partner requests the judge to order partition.
2
State the position of al-Quduri
Al-Quduri holds that only the request of the large-share partner (صاحب کثیر) is accepted because he benefits; the small-share partner's request is rejected as obstinate (متعنت).
Rationale: the first partner benefits, the second seeks his own ruin.
3
State the position of al-Khassaf (or al-Jassas)
Al-Khassaf reverses the ruling: the large-share partner's request is rejected because he intends to harm the other; the small-share partner's request is accepted because he accepts his own loss.
Rationale: intention to harm invalidates request; acceptance of loss validates it.
4
State the position of al-Hakim al-Shahid
Al-Hakim al-Shahid holds that partition should be granted on the request of either partner, as the purpose of partition (benefit) is achieved in both cases.
He sees the proof as supporting both previous positions partially.
5
Identify the relied-upon position (tarjih)
The author of al-Hidayah and later authoritative works prefer al-Quduri's view. This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling for fatwa).
Majma' al-Anhar explicitly states 'علیہ الفتوی' (upon it is the fatwa).

علیہ الفتوی
تنبیہ یہاں جصاص کہا ہے جو ابو بکر جصاص رازگی سے مشہور ہیں اور بعض نسخوں میں خصاف ہے اور یہی صحیح ہے اسلئے کہ یہ خصاف ہی کا ذکر کردہ قول ہے۔ مگر صاحب مجمع الانہر نے قول قدوری کے بعد لکھا ہے وهو الاصح هذا قول الخصاف والامام السرخسی، پھر فرمایا و ذكر الخصاف عكسه، اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ مذہب اول ہی ہے ثانی کے وہ ناقل ہیں۔ بعض حضرات نے قول حاکم شہید کو ترجیح دی ہے مگر لائق ترجیح و معتمد علیہ وہ قول اول ہی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے خصاف کے قول کو نقل نہیں کیا یہ ہمارے قول سابق کا مؤید ہے کہ اس میں دو ہی قول ہیں فتند بر ۔
Two partners: one has a very small share that cannot be benefited from after division, and the other has a large share that can be benefited from after division. One partner requests division. Should the judge divide or not? And if one benefits and the other is harmed due to the smallness of his share, then if the owner of the large share requests, the judge divides; and if the owner of the small share requests, he does not divide, because the first benefits from it so his request is considered, and the second is obstinate in his request so it is not considered. Al-Jassas mentioned the opposite of this, because the owner of the large share intends to harm others and the other is content with harming himself. Al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned in his Mukhtasar that whichever of them requests division, the judge divides, and the meaning is included in what we mentioned. The more correct opinion mentioned in the book is the first. Commentary: There are two partners. One has a very small share that cannot be benefited from after division, and the other has a large share that can be benefited from after division. One of them requests division. What should the judge do? There are three sayings: 1. The saying of al-Quduri: if the requester is the owner of the large share, division will be made; and on the request of the owner of the small share, division will not be made, because the first's request is valid as his benefit remains, and the second's request is invalid because he seeks his own ruin. 2. The saying of al-Jassas: division will not be made on the first's request but will be made on the second's request. The second's request is valid because he is content with his loss, and the first's request is invalid because he seeks to harm the other. 3. The saying of al-Hakim al-Shahid, which he stated in his Mukhtasar: whichever of them requests division, the judge divides. And the proof mentioned previously also serves as proof for this: one part of the proof supports the first saying, the other part supports the second saying. Then the author of al-Hidayah preferred al-Quduri's saying among these three, and in Majma' al-Anhar it is said: upon it is the fatwa. Warning: Here it says al-Jassas, who is well-known as Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi, and in some copies it is al-Khassaf, and this is correct because this saying is actually that of al-Khassaf. But the author of Majma' al-Anhar wrote after al-Quduri's saying: 'it is the more correct; this is the saying of al-Khassaf and Imam al-Sarakhsi.' Then he said: 'al-Khassaf mentioned the opposite of it.' From this it is known that the first school of thought is the one that transmits the second. Some scholars have preferred the saying of al-Hakim al-Shahid, but the more deserving of preference and the reliable one is the first saying. The author of al-Hidayah did not quote al-Khassaf's saying; this supports our previous statement that there are only two sayings, so reflect.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the partition of jointly owned property when one partner's share is so small that after division he cannot benefit from it, while the other partner's share is large enough to be beneficial. The core issue is whether the judge should compel partition when only the partner with the large share requests it, or only when the small-share partner requests it, or regardless of who requests. Three positions are recorded: (1) Al-Quduri holds that only the request of the large-share partner is valid, because he benefits, while the small-share partner's request is considered obstinate. (2) Al-Jassas (or al-Khassaf, as the tanbih clarifies) reverses this: the large-share partner's request is invalid because he intends to harm, while the small-share partner's request is valid because he accepts his own loss. (3) Al-Hakim al-Shahid says partition should be granted on the request of either partner, as the purpose of partition (completing benefit) is achieved in both cases. The author of al-Hidayah (and later authoritative works like Majma' al-Anhar) prefers al-Quduri's position, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. The tanbih corrects a potential confusion: the second position is attributed to al-Khassaf, not al-Jassas, though some manuscripts have al-Jassas. Ultimately, the text concludes that the correct view is al-Quduri's, and that there are effectively only two distinct positions (Quduri and Khassaf), with Hakim al-Shahid's view being subsumed under the previous reasoning.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Partition or division of jointly owned property among co-owners.
انتفاع (intifa'): Benefiting from property in a usable manner after partition.
متعنت (muta'anniṯ): One who is obstinate or seeks to cause harm or waste; here refers to the partner who requests partition despite foreseeing loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, when one partner's share is too small to benefit from after partition and the other's share is large, whose request for partition is accepted?
A) The request of the partner with the small share.
B) The request of the partner with the large share.
C) The request of either partner regardless of share size.
D) The judge decides based on his own discretion.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when partition would make one partner's share unusable, only the partner who benefits from partition may compel it; the other's request is considered obstinate.

Partition When Both Partners Lose Benefit
Ruling on partition with loss of benefit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on partition when both shares are too small, explain the reasoning based on benefit and consent, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference.

Core Idea
Compulsory partition requires benefit; if both lose, only mutual consent allows division.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a practical problem: when dividing a joint property would leave both owners with shares too small to use. The Hanafi ruling balances the purpose of partition (to achieve benefit) with the owners' autonomy. The judge cannot compel division that destroys benefit, but may execute it if both partners consent, trusting their judgment. However, a later Hanafi scholar, Allamah al-Zaylai, holds that even with consent the judge should not divide.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the condition: both co-owners suffer harm because each share is too small to be beneficial after partition.
2
State the Default Ruling
The judge cannot compel partition (jabr) because the legal basis for compulsion is to complete benefit, and here benefit is lost.
Compulsion only valid when partition yields benefit.
3
Understand the Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The purpose of jabr is tahmil al-manfa'ah (achieving benefit). Since partition causes tafwit al-manfa'ah (loss of benefit), jabr is not allowed.
This is the core ta'lil.
4
Identify the Exception: Mutual Consent
If both owners voluntarily consent, the judge may divide because the right belongs to them and they know their own interests. Consent overcomes the apparent harm.
Consent indicates hidden benefit.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Allamah al-Zaylai in al-Tabyin holds that even with mutual consent, the judge should not partition, possibly because the harm is certain.
This is a dissenting view within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شریک کا حصہ اتنا کم ہے کہ قسمت کے بعد اس سے انتفاع ممکن نہیں تو قاضی با بھی رضا کے ساتھ تقسیم کر سکتا ہے ورنہ نہیں
وان كان كل واحد يستضر لصغره لم يقسمها الابتراضيهما لان الجبر على القسمة لتكميل المنفعة وفى هذا تفويتها ويجوز بتراضيهما لان الحق لهما وهما اعرف بشأنهما اما القاضي فيعتمد الظاهر ترجمہ: اور اگر ہر ایک کو ضرر ہو اس کے چھوٹا ہونے کی وجہ سے تو قاضی اس کو تقسیم نہیں کرے گا مگر ان دونوں کی رضامندی کے ساتھ اسلئے کہ قسمت پر جبر تحمیل منفعت کیلئے ہے اور اس میں منفعت کی تفویت ہے اور ان دونوں کی رضامندی سے جائز ہے اسلئے کہ حق ان دونوں کا ہے اور وہ دونوں اپنی شان کو زیادہ جاننے والے ہیں بہر حال قاضی پس وہ ظاہر پر اعتماد کرے گا۔ تشریح یعنی اگر ہر ایک کا حصہ اتنا چھوٹا ہو کہ قسمت کے بعد قابل استفاع نہ رہے تو قاضی اس وقت تقسیم کر سکتا ہے جبکہ دونوں راضی ہو جائیں ورنہ تقسیم نہیں کرے گا۔ کیونکہ قسمت کا موضوع یہ تھا کہ اس سے منفعت کی تکمیل ہو اور یہاں منفعت کی تفویت ہے۔ ہاں اگر دونوں راضی ہوں تو قاضی تقسیم کر دے گا کیونکہ انہیں دونوں کا حق ہے اور اپنے معاملات و حالات وہ خوب جانتے ہیں۔ البتہ قاضی کا کام ظاہر پر اعتماد کرنا ہے اور ظاہر ضرر اور نقصان ہے لہذا ایک کی طلب پر ظاہر کا اعتبار کرے گا اور دونوں کی طلب ظاہر کو بدلنے والی ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ ان دونوں کی آمادگی یہ بتاتی ہے کہ شاید اس میں کچھ منفعت ہو۔
علامہ زیلعی نے تبیین میں کہا ہے کہ قاضی دونوں کی طلب کے اوپر بھی تقسیم نہیں کرے گا۔
One partner's share is so small that after partition it is not possible to benefit from it. Then the judge can divide only with their mutual consent, otherwise not. And if each one is harmed due to its smallness, he will not divide it except with their mutual consent, because compulsion on partition is for the completion of benefit, and in this there is loss of benefit. It is permissible with their mutual consent because the right belongs to them and they know their own situation better. The judge relies on apparent circumstances. Commentary: That is, if each one's share is so small that after partition it is not usable for benefit, then the judge can divide only when both are pleased; otherwise he will not divide. Because the purpose of partition was the completion of benefit, and here there is loss of benefit. Yes, if both are pleased, the judge will divide because the right belongs to both of them, and they know their own matters and conditions well. However, the judge's job is to rely on apparent circumstances, and the apparent is harm and loss. Therefore, on the request of one, he will rely on the apparent, and the request of both changes the apparent, because their readiness indicates that perhaps there is some benefit in it. Allamah al-Zaylai said in al-Tabyin that the judge will not divide even on the request of both.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling (hukm) on partition when both co-owners suffer because their shares are too small to be beneficial after division. The principle is that compulsion to partition (jabr) is justified only to achieve benefit (tahmil al-manfa'ah). If partition results in loss of benefit (tafwit al-manfa'ah), the judge cannot compel it. However, if both partners voluntarily agree, the judge may execute the partition because the right to partition belongs to them, and they are better aware of their own interests. The judge's default stance is to rely on the outward appearance (al-zahir), which suggests harm. When both request, that outward appearance is overcome by their consent, implying some hidden benefit. The passage also records a dissenting view from Allamah al-Zaylai in al-Tabyin that even with mutual request, the judge should not partition, possibly because the harm remains certain. But the main text allows partition with consent.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr): Compulsion by the judge to enforce partition against a co-owner's will.
رضا (riḍā): Mutual consent of all co-owners to partition despite apparent loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When both partners' shares are too small to benefit from after partition, under what condition can the judge divide the property?
A) Only if one partner requests.
B) Only if both partners consent.
C) Under no condition is division allowed.
D) Only if the property is movable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi judge cannot compel partition that destroys benefit for both, but may execute it upon mutual consent; however, some scholars like al-Zaylai dissent, arguing that even with consent the judge should not divide.

Partition of Homogeneous Movable Goods
Ruling on partition of goods of the same type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for compulsory partition of movable goods in Hanafi fiqh and explain the rational proof for it.

Core Idea
Homogeneous goods are divisible by the judge because unity of genus unifies the partners' purpose.

This ruling addresses a practical scenario in co-ownership: when partners disagree over division of movable goods. The Hanafi school provides a clear criterion: if the goods are all of the same type, the judge can enforce partition. The reasoning is based on the unity of purpose—since each partner wants their share of the same thing, division is straightforward and equitable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property type
Confirm that the goods are movable (العروض) and of a single type (صنف واحد). Homogeneity is key.
Goods of the same genus, e.g., wheat, sheep, or identical manufactured items.
2
State the ruling
The judge may partition the goods upon a partner's request. The division is valid and binding.
This is a sahih (valid) act; the judge is authorized to do so.
3
Explain the rational proof
Unity of genus (اتحاد الجنس) leads to unity of purpose (اتحاد المقصود). This ensures equity (التعديل) and completion of benefit (التكميل في المنفعة) for all partners.
The proof is aqli (rational): no need for valuation or conversion, as each unit is equivalent.
4
Note the practical implication
If goods are heterogeneous, forced partition may not be possible; partners may need to consent or sell and divide proceeds.
This lesson focuses on the homogeneous case; the reverse is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منقول اشیاء میں شرکاء نے تقسیم کا مطالبہ کیا اور جنس بھی ایک ہی ہے تو قاضی تقسیم کرے
قال ويقسم العروض اذا كانت من صنف واحد لان عند اتحاد الجنس يتحد المقصود فيحصل التعديل في القسمة والتكميل في المنفعة
In movable goods, if the partners request division and the genus is also one, then the judge divides. He (the author) said: 'And he divides the goods when they are of one type, because when the genus is unified, the purpose is unified, so equity is achieved in the division and completion of benefit.'
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a succinct ruling from the original text (likely from al-Hidayah). It addresses the case where the property to be partitioned consists of movable goods (العروض) of a single type (صنف واحد). The ruling is that the judge should proceed with partition when the co-owners request it. The proof (dalil) is rational: when the goods are homogeneous, the purpose of all partners is identical (attainment of their share), so division can be done equitably and each partner can benefit from their portion. This contrasts with heterogeneous goods where division might be difficult or inequitable. The statement "فيحصل التعديل في القسمة والتكميل في المنفعة" indicates that partition achieves both equity (al-ta'dil) and completion of benefit (al-takmil al-manfa'ah). This is a straightforward ruling without ikhtilaf in the text.

Key Terms
عروض (ʿurūḍ): Movable goods, including merchandise and commodities.
صنف واحد (ṣinf wāḥid): Single type or category of goods, homogeneous in nature.
تعديل (taʿdīl): Equitable adjustment; ensuring fairness in division.
تكمل (takmīl): Completion; fulfillment of benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the goods are movable.
B) Because the partners have consented.
C) Because unity of genus unifies the purpose, enabling equity and benefit.
D) Because the judge has discretion.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, partition of movable goods of the same type is valid because unity of genus ensures equitable division and full benefit.

Tarjih of Quduri's Statement on Partition with Minimal Shares
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partition with small shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi statements on partition when shares are too small, explain the tarjih process, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When shares are too small for benefit, the judge cannot force partition without consent – that is the mufta bihi Hanafi view.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a judge can force partition when each partner's share is so small that it yields no benefit. There are three reported statements, and the later authorities have given preference to the view of Al-Quduri. Understanding this tarjih illuminates how the Hanafi school resolves internal differences and establishes the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three statements
List the three positions on partition when shares are too small: (1) Quduri: judge cannot force partition without consent; (2) Hakim Shahid: judge can force partition; (3) third variant (often attributed to Khassaf).
Some authorities consider only two statements, as Al-Hidayah omits the third.
2
Understand the basis of each
Forced partition is normally for the completion of benefit (takmil al-manfa'a). When benefit is lost due to smallness, some scholars say the basis for forced partition disappears, requiring consent; others still allow it.
The proof mentioned earlier (from a previous passage) supports both sides depending on which part is emphasized.
3
Trace the tarjih process
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah explicitly prefers Quduri's statement. Later works like Majma' al-Anhur confirm that the fatwa is upon Quduri's view, citing Durar and Dhakheera.
Tarjih is based on the principle that the ruling should align with the absence of benefit.
4
Note the textual correction
The name 'Jassas' in some manuscripts is a scribal error for 'Khassaf'. The statement belongs to Khassaf and Imam Sarakhsi, not Jassas.
This correction affects attributions but not the legal conclusion.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the judge should not partition except with the consent of both partners. Some scholars preferred Hakim Shahid, but the mu'tamad (reliable) view is the first.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور ماقبل میں ذکر کردہ دلیل اس کی بھی دلیل ہے یعنی دلیل کے ایک پرزے میں قول اول کی دلیل کام کرے گی ۔ اور دوسرے پرزے میں قول ثانی کی۔ پھر صاحب ہدایہ نے ان اقوال ثلاثہ میں قول قدوری کو تر جیح دی ہے۔ اور مجمع الا نہر ص ۴۷۲ ج ۲ پر بحوالہ در و ذخیرہ کہا ہے ؛ علیہ الفتوی تنبیہ یہاں جصاص کہا ہے جو ابو بکر جصاص رازگی سے مشہور ہیں اور بعض نسخوں میں خصاف ہے اور یہی صحیح ہے اسلئے کہ یہ خصاف ہی کا ذکر کردہ قول ہے۔ مگر صاحب مجمع الانہر نے قول قدوری کے بعد لکھا ہے وهو الاصح هذا قول الخصاف والامام السرخسی، پھر فرمایا و ذكر الخصاف عكسه، اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ مذہب اول ہی ہے ثانی کے وہ ناقل ہیں۔ بعض حضرات نے قول حاکم شہید کو ترجیح دی ہے مگر لائق ترجیح و معتمد علیہ وہ قول اول ہی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے خصاف کے قول کو نقل نہیں کیا یہ ہمارے قول سابق کا مؤید ہے کہ اس میں دو ہی قول ہیں فتند بر ۔
And the proof mentioned previously is also proof for this, meaning in one part of the proof the first statement's proof will work, and in the second part the second statement's proof. Then the author of Hidayah gave preference to Quduri's statement among these three statements. And in Majma' al-Anhur vol. 2 p. 472, citing Durar and Dhakheera, it is said: 'The fatwa is upon it.' Warning: Here 'Jassas' is said, who is famous as Abu Bakr Jassas Razi, and in some copies it is 'Khassaf', and this is correct because this is the statement mentioned by Khassaf. But the author of Majma' al-Anhur wrote after Quduri's statement: 'And it is the most correct, this is the statement of Khassaf and Imam Sarakhsi.' Then he said: 'And Khassaf mentioned the opposite of it.' From this it is understood that the first madhhab is the same; he is a narrator of the second. Some people preferred the statement of Hakim Shahid, but worthy of preference and relied upon is the first statement. The author of Hidayah did not narrate Khassaf's statement; this supports our previous statement that there are only two statements here, so consider.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the correct position among three statements on partition when shares are so small that benefit is lost. The commentator begins by noting that the proof mentioned earlier supports both the first and second statements in different parts. Then he states that the author of Hidayah (Al-Marghinani) gave preference (tarjih) to the statement of Al-Quduri. He supports this by citing the later authority Majma' al-Anhur, which declares that the fatwa is upon Quduri's view. The passage includes a textual note (tanbih) clarifying that the name mentioned in some copies is 'Jassas' (Abu Bakr al-Jassas) but the correct name is 'Khassaf', as the statement belongs to Khassaf. However, the commentator reconciles this by noting that Majma' al-Anhur attributes the relied-upon view to both Khassaf and Imam Sarakhsi. He also mentions that some scholars preferred the statement of Hakim Shahid, but the most worthy and relied-upon (mu'tamad) remains the first statement (Quduri's). He concludes that the author of Hidayah's omission of Khassaf's statement supports the idea that there are only two statements here, not three. The authority weight of this tarjih is from later fatawa (Fatawa Mutakhkhirin) as it cites Majma' al-Anhur.

Key Terms
ترجیح (tarjih): The act of preferring one legal opinion over another based on strength of evidence.
علیہ الفتوی (alayhi al-fatwa): The relied-upon position upon which fatwa is given.
جصاص (Jassas): A reference to Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi, a Hanafi scholar; but here noted as a textual error for 'Khassaf'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in this passage, when can a judge partition property if each partner's share is too small for benefit?
A) The judge can partition by force even if one partner objects.
B) The judge can only partition with the consent of both partners.
C) The judge cannot partition at all.
D) The judge must refer the matter to a higher court.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position on partition of property with shares too small for benefit is that the judge cannot force partition without the consent of all partners.

intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Forced Partition When Benefit Is Lost
Ruling on forced partition when shares are too small to be beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule on forced partition when shares are too small to be beneficial, and identify when consent overrides the apparent harm.

Core Idea
Forced partition is to complete benefit; if partition destroys benefit, the judge cannot force it.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses a practical problem in co-ownership: what happens when a forced partition would leave each partner with a share too small to be useful? The text provides a clear ruling based on the underlying purpose of compulsory partition. Let us unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine the key facts: one or both partners have shares so small that after partition the shares provide no benefit.
The scenario is about harm (darar) due to the smallness of the share.
2
State the ruling
The judge cannot force partition without the consent of both partners. If both consent, the judge may partition.
This is the default Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Quduri).
3
Understand the dalil (proof)
The proof is qiyas based on the illah (effective cause): forced partition is legislated for the completion of benefit (takmil al-manfa'ah). In this case, partition results in loss of benefit (tafwit), so the illah is absent, making forced partition impermissible.
The dalil is explicitly stated: 'لان الجبر على القسمة لتكميل المنفعة وفى هذا تفويتها'.
4
Examine the exception of mutual consent
If both partners consent, the judge may partition because the right belongs to them and they know their own interests. Their consent overrides the apparent harm (zahir).
The judge relies on the apparent (zahir); mutual consent changes that apparent indication.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Zayla'i (in Al-Tabyin) holds a stricter view: the judge should not partition even with mutual consent. However, the relied-upon position is the more permissive one (Quduri and Abu Hanifa).
This ikhtilaf is noted in the commentary but the text prefers the first view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شریک کا حصہ اتنا کم ہے کہ قسمت کے بعد اس سے انتفاع ممکن نہیں تو قاضی با بھی رضا کے ساتھ تقسیم کر سکتا ہے ورنہ نہیں وان كان كل واحد يستضر لصغره لم يقسمها الابتراضيهما لان الجبر على القسمة لتكميل المنفعة وفى هذا تفويتها ويجوز بتراضيهما لان الحق لهما وهما اعرف بشأنهما اما القاضي فيعتمد الظاهر ترجمہ: اور اگر ہر ایک کو ضرر ہو اس کے چھوٹا ہونے کی وجہ سے تو قاضی اس کو تقسیم نہیں کرے گا مگر ان دونوں کی رضامندی کے ساتھ اسلئے کہ قسمت پر جبر تحمیل منفعت کیلئے ہے اور اس میں منفعت کی تفویت ہے اور ان دونوں کی رضامندی سے جائز ہے اسلئے کہ حق ان دونوں کا ہے اور وہ دونوں اپنی شان کو زیادہ جاننے والے ہیں بہر حال قاضی پس وہ ظاہر پر اعتماد کرے گا۔ تشریح یعنی اگر ہر ایک کا حصہ اتنا چھوٹا ہو کہ قسمت کے بعد قابل استفاع نہ رہے تو قاضی اس وقت تقسیم کر سکتا ہے جبکہ دونوں راضی ہو جائیں ورنہ تقسیم نہیں کرے گا۔ کیونکہ قسمت کا موضوع یہ تھا کہ اس سے منفعت کی تکمیل ہو اور یہاں منفعت کی تفویت ہے۔ ہاں اگر دونوں راضی ہوں تو قاضی تقسیم کر دے گا کیونکہ انہیں دونوں کا حق ہے اور اپنے معاملات و حالات وہ خوب جانتے ہیں۔ البتہ قاضی کا کام ظاہر پر اعتماد کرنا ہے اور ظاہر ضرر اور نقصان ہے لہذا ایک کی طلب پر ظاہر کا اعتبار کرے گا اور دونوں کی طلب ظاہر کو بدلنے والی ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ ان دونوں کی آمادگی یہ بتاتی ہے کہ شاید اس میں کچھ منفعت ہو۔ علامہ زیلعی نے تبیین میں کہا ہے کہ قاضی دونوں کی طلب کے اوپر بھی تقسیم نہیں کرے گا۔
If the share of one partner is so small that after partition benefiting from it is not possible, then the judge can partition with consent only, otherwise not. And if each one is harmed due to its smallness, he will not partition it except with their consent, because forced partition is for the completion of benefit, and in this there is loss of benefit. And it is permissible with their consent because the right is theirs and they know better about their situation. As for the judge, he relies on the apparent. Translation: And if each one is harmed due to its smallness, then the judge will not partition it except with their consent, because forced partition is for the completion of benefit, and in this there is loss of benefit. And with their consent it is permissible because the right is theirs and they know better about their own affairs. In any case, the judge relies on the apparent. Explanation: That is, if each one's share is so small that after partition it is not usable, then the judge can partition only when both consent; otherwise he will not partition. Because the subject of partition was that through it benefit is completed, and here there is loss of benefit. Yes, if both consent, then the judge will partition because the right belongs to both and they know their own matters and conditions well. However, the judge's job is to rely on the apparent, and the apparent is harm and loss, so on the request of one he will consider the apparent, and the request of both will change the apparent because their willingness indicates that perhaps there is some benefit. Allamah Zayla'i said in Tabyin that the judge will not partition even upon the request of both.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its proof regarding partition (qismah) of property where the shares are so small that after division they provide no benefit. The ruling has two scenarios. First, if one partner's share is too small to be beneficial, the judge can partition only if both consent. Second, if both partners suffer harm because each share is small, the judge does not partition except with their mutual consent. The proof (dalil) given is that forced partition (jabr 'ala al-qismah) is legislated to complete benefit; here, forcing partition would negate that benefit. The exception is mutual consent because the right belongs to the partners and they know their own interests best. The judge relies on the apparent (zahir), which is harm, so on the request of one partner alone, the judge will not force it. But if both request, their willingness changes the apparent, suggesting hidden benefit. The commentator notes an additional opinion from Allamah Zayla'i in Al-Tabyin that the judge should not partition even when both request; this represents a stricter view. The authority weight is from the commentary on Hidayah, drawing from earlier Hanafi sources. The hukm level is one of permissibility with conditions; the judge is not compelled to force partition.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr): Forcing a party to accept a transaction (here, partition) by judicial authority.
تفویت (tafwit): Loss or negation of benefit; the opposite of takmil al-manfa'ah (completion of benefit).
ظاہر (zahir): The apparent external circumstance (here, visible harm) that the judge relies on.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the judge not force partition when each partner's share is too small for benefit?
A) Because forced partition requires a higher authority.
B) Because forced partition is for the completion of benefit, which is absent here.
C) Because the partners must first agree on a valuation.
D) Because the property is likely to be indivisible.

Key Takeaway
Forced partition is grounded in benefit; when partition destroys benefit, it cannot be forced except with mutual consent.

Forced Partition of Movables of the Same Genus
Ruling that a judge can force partition of movable property of the same genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for forced partition of movables and apply it to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A judge can force partition only when movables are of the same genus, ensuring equal value.

Co-ownership of movable property can lead to disputes when one partner demands partition but the other refuses. Hanafi law allows the judge to force partition, but only under a key condition: the property must be of a single genus (sinnf wahid). This lesson unpacks that condition and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is forced partition (qismah jabran) of movable property ('urud). The ruling is that the judge may (or must, upon request) impose partition.
This is a judicial power, not automatic.
2
Pinpoint the condition
The condition is that the movables must be of the same genus (min sinnf wahid). This means they are homogeneous in type and value, e.g., all wheat of similar quality.
'Urf (custom) determines what constitutes one genus.
3
Understand the rationale
When the genus is unified, the purpose (al-maqsud) is unified. This allows for equity (ta'dil) in division and full benefit (takmil al-manfa'ah) to each partner.
Equity means shares are equal in value; benefit means each share is usable.
4
Recognize the authority
The ruling is from Al-Quduri (a foundational Hanafi matn) and is part of Zahir al-Riwayah. It is widely accepted among Hanafis.
Other schools may have different conditions.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Test with an example: Two partners jointly own 100 sacks of rice of the same grade. One demands partition. The judge can force partition because the rice is one genus. If the property were mixed (e.g., rice and wheat), forced partition is not allowed.
The key is homogeneity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منقول اشیاء میں شرکاء نے تقسیم کا مطالبہ کیا اور جنس بھی ایک ہی ہے تو قاضی تقسیم کرے

قال ويقسم العروض اذا كانت من صنف واحد لان عند اتحاد الجنس يتحد المقصود فيحصل التعديل في القسمة والتكميل في المنفعة

ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور سامانوں کو قاضی تقسیم کرے گا ( جبراً) جبکہ ایک جنس سے ہوں اسلئے کہ اتحاد جنس کے وقت مقصود متحد ہے تو قسمت میں تعدیل اور منفعت میں تکمیل حاصل ہو جائے گی۔
Among movable things, the partners demand partition and the genus is also the same, so the judge will partition. He (Al-Quduri) said: And he divides the commodities when they are from one kind, because when the genus is unified, the purpose is unified, so equity in partition and completeness in benefit are achieved. Translation: Al-Quduri said: The judge will partition (by force) the commodities when they are from one kind, because when the genus is unified, the purpose is unified, so equity in partition and completeness in benefit are achieved.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that when movable property (al-‘urud) is of a single genus (e.g., all cloth, all gold coins), the judge can force partition if one partner requests it. The source quoted is from Al-Quduri: the judge divides commodities if they are of one type. The rationale (not fully explicit here but given in the Arabic) is that when the genus is unified, the purpose (al-maqsud) is unified, and thus equality (ta'dil) in division and completion of benefit (takmil al-manfa'ah) are attained. This means the shares can be made equal in value, and each partner gets a part that is genuinely beneficial. The judge's power of jabr (forcing) applies because there is no harm—each gets a usable share. This is a standard Hanafi rule: forced partition is allowed when the property is homogeneous and divisible without loss of value.

Key Terms
عروض (‘urud): Movable property, commodities (excluding real estate and certain items).
صنف واحد (sinnf wahid): One species/type; same genus (jins) in value and nature.
تعدیل (ta‘dil): Equity in division; ensuring each share is equal in value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from Al-Quduri, what is required for a judge to force partition of movables?
A) The movables must be of high value.
B) The movables must be of the same genus.
C) The partners must be present in court.
D) The movables must be immovable property.

Key Takeaway
A judge can only force partition of movables when they are of the same genus, ensuring equity and full benefit.

Partition of Different Genera Prohibited
Ruling with proof: judge cannot force partition of different genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judge cannot force partition of different genera and explain the distinction between tamyiz and mu'awadah.

Core Idea
A judge cannot force partition of different genera; it becomes an exchange requiring mutual consent.

This lesson examines a specific limitation on the judge's power in partition (qismah). The source clarifies that partition is fundamentally a process of distinguishing (tamyiz) each partner's share. When property is of different types, no true tamyiz occurs; instead, any division is an exchange (mu'awadah). Exchange is a contract that requires the free consent of both parties, and a judge cannot compel consent. This ruling underscores the Hanafi principle that forced division only applies to homogeneous property where shares can be materially separated without valuation or barter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is a judge forcing partition of two different genera (e.g., camels and horses) into shares. The ruling is that it is not permissible (haram) for the judge to do so.
2
Recognize the illah (cause)
The text states 'because there is no mixing between the two genera' (la ikhtilat bayn al-jinsayn). This absence of mixing is the effective cause that changes the nature of the division.
Ikhtilat is the legal reason that necessitates partition in the first place.
3
Understand the consequence: mu'awadah
Since there is no mixing, allocating one type to one partner and another to the other is not true partition (tamyiz) but an exchange (mu'awadah). The shares are already distinct; any division is a transfer of ownership between the partners.
4
Apply the principle: exchange requires consent
The text says 'its way is mutual consent, not the judge's force' (sabiluha al-taradi duna jabr al-qadi). Exchange is a contract that requires consent from both parties. The judge's authority (jabr) does not extend to forcing a sale or barter.
5
Note the practical boundary
The judge can only enforce partition when the property is homogeneous and the division results in pure tamyiz without any exchange element. This ruling confirms that forced partition is limited to cases of mixing within a single genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاضی دو جنسوں تقسیم نہیں کر سکتا

ولا يقسم الجنسين بعضها في بعض لانه لا اختلاط بين الجنسين فلا تقع القسمة تمييز ابل تقع معاوضة وسبيلها التراضي دون جبر القاضي

ترجمہ اور قاضی دو جنسوں کو تقسیم نہیں کرے گا ان میں سے بعض کو بعض کے اندر اسلئے کہ دوجنسوں کے درمیان اختلاط نہیں ہوتا تو قسمت تمیز واقع نہیں ہوگی بلکہ وہ معاوضہ واقع ہوگا اور معاوضہ کی سبیل تراضی ہے نہ کہ جبر قاضی ۔
The judge cannot partition two genera. And he does not divide the two genera, some of them into the other, because there is no mixing between the two genera, so partition as distinguishing does not occur, but rather it occurs as exchange, and its way is mutual consent, not the judge's force. Translation: And the judge will not divide two genera (by force) some into some, because between two genera there is no mixing, so partition as distinguishing will not occur, but rather it will occur as exchange, and the way of exchange is mutual consent, not the judge's force.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its proof: a judge cannot force partition when the property consists of two different genera (e.g., camels and horses, or different types of goods). The reason given is that there is no mixing (ikhtilat) between the two types; true partition is a process of distinguishing (tamyiz) the shares, but here the shares are naturally distinct. Any allocation of one type to one partner and another to the other is not a true division but an exchange (mu'awadah), and exchange requires mutual consent (taradi). The judge's power of jabr does not extend to compelling an exchange. Therefore, partners must agree among themselves. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that forced partition is only allowed when the property is homogeneous and the division results in true tamyiz without need for valuation across types.

Key Terms
تمییز (tamyiz): Distinguishing the shares through partition; the legal goal of qismah.
معاوضہ (mu'awadah): Exchange (barter/sale) where each party gives and receives different property.
اختلاط (ikhtilat): Mixing of shares physically or legally, necessitating division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can't a judge force partition of property consisting of two different genera?
A) Because the partners are likely to disagree.
B) Because it would become an exchange requiring mutual consent.
C) Because the judge lacks the expertise to value them.
D) Because the property is likely immeasurable.

Key Takeaway
A judge may only force partition when property is homogeneous and shares are mixed; otherwise, division is an exchange requiring consent.
